Classification of Motion: Rectilinear, Circular, and Composite
Al-Razi classifies motion into three types and states Plato's view on rectilinear motion ending.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of motion (rectilinear, circular, composite) and state the physical principle that rectilinear motion ends in rest.

Core Idea
Motion in Islamic natural philosophy is either rectilinear, circular, or composite; each type determines whether a body moves perpetually or comes to rest.

This passage from Al-Razi's Mulakhkhas lays out a fundamental classification of motion that underpins much of Islamic natural philosophy and kalam. Understanding the three types of motion and the principle that rectilinear motion always ends in rest is essential for grasping arguments about the contingency of the world and the nature of celestial and sublunar bodies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of motion
Find in the Arabic the three categories: مستديرة (circular), مستقيمة (rectilinear), and مركبة منهما (composite of both). Note the examples: عملة (screw) and كرة متوجة (rotating sphere).
2
Understand the motive force principle
The passage states that motion continues as long as the motive force (قوة محركة) remains in the body, and it stops only when an impediment exists. That impediment may be removed by itself or by an external cause.
3
Relate each type to its physical context
Rectilinear motion is typical of sublunar bodies and ends in rest; circular motion is typical of celestial spheres and is perpetual; composite motion combines both, as in mechanical devices.
4
Note the Plato-Aristotle disagreement
The passage attributes to Plato the view that every rectilinear motion ends in rest, contrary to Aristotle. This disagreement highlights different cosmological assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقيدات الشرطية: أن القوة المحركة للجسم إذا كانت باقية فيه فلا يقف إلا لوجود ما يعوق الطبيعة عن الترك. وذلك المعوق إما أن يعدم لذاته وإما أن يعدم بباعث خارجي. وبيان ذلك: أن كل حركة مستقيمة تنتهي إلى وقوف، وهذا مذهب أفلاطون خلافاً لأرسطو. والحركة قد تكون مستقيمة، وقد تكون مستديرة، وقد تكون مركبة منهما كحركة العملة والكرة المتوجة.
Conditional restrictions: The motive force in a body, if it remains in it, does not stop except due to the existence of something that impedes the nature from [continuing] motion. That impediment either ceases by itself or ceases due to an external cause. Explanation: Every rectilinear motion ends in rest; this is the doctrine of Plato, contrary to Aristotle. Motion may be rectilinear, may be circular, and may be composite of both, like the motion of a screw and a rotating sphere.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage classifies motion into three types and presents a physical principle about constant motion. The author first states that motion is either rectilinear, circular, or a composite of the two, giving examples from mechanics (screw and rotating sphere). He then addresses the condition for uninterrupted motion: the motive force remaining in the moving body; it only stops when an obstacle intervenes. That obstacle may be removed intrinsically or by an external cause. The claim that every rectilinear motion ends in rest is attributed to Plato, contrasting with Aristotle's view that the universe may allow perpetual rectilinear motion? This classification is foundational for understanding physical motion in Islamic natural philosophy, as it distinguishes the sublunar (rectilinear) from the celestial (circular) motion, a central point in kalam arguments about the contingency of the world. The example of a screw (عملة) shows mixed motion used in mechanics, and a rotating sphere (كرة متوجة) illustrates pure circular motion.

Key Takeaway
The classification of motion into rectilinear, circular, and composite is foundational to Islamic natural philosophy, with rectilinear motion always ending in rest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Dilemma of Time: Stable or Passing?
Author presents and refutes two alternatives for the existence of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a dilemma argument and evaluate its refutations using the example of time's existence.

Core Idea
Dilemmas force us to reject both horns, but the arguments must be valid.

In this passage, the author presents a classic dilemma about the nature of time. Either time is stable or it is passing. Both alternatives are shown to be impossible. This logical move—presenting exhaustive options and then eliminating each—is a powerful tool in Islamic dialectics (jadal). We will dissect the reasoning and check whether the refutations hold.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dilemma structure
The author sets up two exhaustive alternatives for time's existence: time must be either stable (mustaqirr) or passing (munqadin). Recognize that this is a disjunction that claims to cover all possibilities.
In jadal, a valid dilemma must have exhaustive and mutually exclusive horns.
2
Examine the refutation of the first horn (stable time)
The author argues that if time were stable, this very day would be identical to the day of the flood (a past event). Since that is impossible, the first horn is rejected. Understand the absurdity: stable time would collapse temporal distinctions.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the horn leads to an impossible consequence.
3
Examine the refutation of the second horn (passing time)
The author claims that a passing (flowing) time leads to infinite regress (tasalsul). In Islamic philosophy, infinite regress is generally rejected. Assess why a succession of moments would require an endless chain, which is considered invalid.
The argument assumes that a flow of time would require a time to measure the flow, and so on.
4
Evaluate the dilemma's logical strength
Are the two alternatives truly exhaustive? Could time be something else, like a mental construct? Could the refutations be flawed? Consider if there might be a third option or if the refutations rely on hidden assumptions.
A strong dilemma withstands scrutiny; a weak one may be rebutted by denying the disjunction or by attacking one refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ففي وجود الزمان: أما تعلم بالضرورة أنا لا نعرف أول الأمر وجوده كما نعرف أن إمكانا [؟] دو هو؟ وإن كان الأمر كذلك... ثم اختلف الناس. فمن قال: إنه موجود. فلا يخلو إما أن يكون مستقرا أو منقضيا. الأول محال، لأن هذا اليوم بعينه هو يوم الطوفان، فكون الحادث في هذا اليوم حادثا في يوم الطوفان محال. والثاني محال أيضا، لأنه يؤدي إلى التسلسل.
Regarding the existence of time: Do you not know by necessity that we do not initially know its existence as we know that possibility [is]? And if it is so... then people differed. Some said: it exists. It must be either stable or passing. The first is impossible, because this very day is the day of the flood, so the occurrence of an event on this day being an occurrence on the day of the flood is impossible. The second is also impossible, because it leads to an infinite regress.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument about the existence of time. The author begins by acknowledging that we have an initial awareness of time, but then notes that philosophers have disagreed on its status. The argument proceeds as a dilemma: if time exists, it must be either static (مستقر) or flowing (منقضيا). The first alternative is refuted by the absurdity that the present day would be identical to the day of the flood (a past event), which is impossible. The second alternative is rejected because a flowing time would require an infinite regress of times. This is a classic logical refutation using reduction to absurdity. The passage does not yet resolve the dilemma, setting up further discussion.

Key Terms
زمان
مستقر
منقض
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because time is always changing.
B) Because it would make the present day identical to a past day, which is absurd.
C) Because stability contradicts the definition of time as measure of motion.
D) Because it leads to an infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
A dilemma argument presents two exhaustive options and refutes each, aiming to show the initial claim is false or needs redefinition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a dilemma argument on a different theological topic: e.g., either the Quran is created or uncreated. Show how one might refute both options.
?
Identify the dilemma in the following passage: 'The world must be either eternal or originated. If eternal, then... If originated, then... Therefore, the world is neither.' Explain which horn is refuted and how.

The Dialectical Proof of the Indivisible Point of Contact
Author proves contact point is indivisible and addresses objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical exchange between the author and his objector concerning the sphere's contact point and evaluate the logical validity of the responses.

Core Idea
The sphere's contact point is indivisible; the dialectical method tests this claim through geometric and temporal objections.

This passage from a natural philosophy text uses a dialectical structure to argue that the point of contact between a sphere and a plane must be indivisible. The author first provides a geometric proof, then anticipates and refutes several objections. Understanding this exchange will sharpen your ability to follow dialectical arguments in Islamic sciences, particularly in kalam and falsafa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main claim (mudda'i)
What is the author trying to prove about the sphere's contact point? Locate the assertion that the place of contact is indivisible.
The claim is that the contact point is an indivisible atom, not a line or area.
2
Map the geometric proof
The author constructs a geometric argument involving perpendiculars and triangles. Follow the reasoning: from the center to the base, the perpendicular is shorter than the two radius lines, contradicting the fact that all radii are equal. This contradiction supports the claim.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Extract the first objection (mu'tarid)
The objector argues that if contact is at a point, then points compose lines, leading to infinite regress. Find this objection in the text.
The objector is likely using the problem of composition of continua.
4
Analyze the author's response (jawab)
How does the author reply? He asserts that the contact point is indivisible and that the sphere's motion does not require a line of contact. Also, he argues about the temporal instant of contact.
The response uses the concept of beginning of non-contact and the necessity of an intermediate instant.
5
Evaluate the dialectic
Assess whether the response successfully refutes the objection. Does the author avoid the infinite regress? Is the temporal argument sound?
The dialectical structure is typical of Islamic jadal, where each objection must be met with a valid counterargument.

The place of contact... We came out from the center of the circle to the base of the arc of the circle. A perpendicular erected on it: the two angles resulting from the sides of the perpendicular, right angles, divide that triangle into two right triangles. The two extreme lines are... for the two great angles, and the perpendicular that occurs at the acute [angle] is greater than the hypotenuse of the acute [angle]. So the perpendicular line is shorter than the two extreme lines, even though the three lines go from the center to the circumference. This is a contradiction. So it is established that the place of contact is indivisible. When we roll the sphere on the surface until completion, there is no doubt that the contact occurs at a point; the contact occurred at a point and there is nothing between the two points. The state at the contact occurring at the first moment of contact with the first point is like the state at the contact with the first point. So the line is composed of those points. If the line is composed of points, then points are also composed of lines and surfaces from the concatenation of surfaces. So the place of contact of the sphere is something indivisible that arises from the joining of its likes, the [؟] and magnitudes, and it is the indivisible part. ... They said: 'We concede that, but the point exists in quantity only through contact, so when it ceases, those points pass away and others occur, so one point never remains.' ... If we concede that the contact occurs at a point, why is it not possible to say that between every two points there is a line? We answer... the impossibility of the existence of a non-straight sphere... ... His statement: 'It is always a single point' – I say: we do not concede that. If in the sphere there is a line that ends actually, it meets the surface. Then when contact ceases, contact occurs at another point, so the second point is actual due to contact, and the first is actual because it is an actual endpoint. ... His statement: 'Why is it not said that between two points there is a line?' Because when contact occurs at the first point, there occurs there... the two times: one for contact and the other for non-contact. Non-contact is motion and does not occur in an instant, while non-contact is part of what occurs now and continues in all time after it. So it is established that non-contact has a beginning that occurs in it. So the instant which is the first time of non-contact is either the instant in which contact occurred or something else. The first is false, because then the sphere in one instant would be with one point in contact and not in contact with the surface, and that is a contradiction. So it must be something else. Then there must be a time between these two instants. If there is, then the sphere in that time would be non-contact with the first point, so it would be stationary.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument in natural philosophy, likely from a section on motion and contact. The author first attempts to prove that the point of contact between a sphere and a plane must be indivisible (an atom). The proof uses a geometric construction: from the center of the sphere to the base of the arc, a perpendicular is drawn, yielding two right triangles. The author argues that the perpendicular is shorter than the two radius lines, which contradicts the fact that all three lines from the center to the circumference are equal. This contradiction shows that the contact point cannot be a line or extension, so it must be a single indivisible point. Then the author considers the case of rolling: as the sphere rolls, the point of contact changes. The objector argues that if contact is at a point, then points would be composed of lines and surfaces, leading to infinite regress. The author responds by affirming that the contact point is indivisible and that the sphere's motion does not require a line of contact. Further objections and responses follow: whether the sphere touches at a point or a line, whether one point can exist continuously, and the nature of time during motion. The author uses the idea that non-contact has a beginning and that there must be an instant between contact and non-contact, otherwise the sphere would be simultaneously in contact and not in contact, which is absurd. This leads to the conclusion that the sphere must be stationary at the moment of transition. The argument is intricate and relies on Aristotelian notions of time, motion, and continuity.

Al-Razi's Reductio Against Points
Author uses motion paradox to refute actual existence of points.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Razi's reductio ad absurdum argument against the actual existence of points and identify its conditional reasoning steps.

Core Idea
Al-Razi uses contradictions from motion to prove points exist only in the mind.

This passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhaṣ critically examines the ontological status of points, a key concept in physics and geometry. He employs a reductio ad absurdum based on motion and contact to show that if points were actual external entities, absurdities would follow. Your goal is to trace his logical steps and see how conditional arguments ('if...then...') lead to the conclusion that points are merely mental constructs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the assumption under attack
Al-Razi assumes for argument that points exist actually in the external world. This is the premise he intends to refute.
The assumption is implicit: we will derive a contradiction from it.
2
Set up the motion scenario
He considers a sphere moving on a surface. Contact between sphere and surface occurs at points. If points are actual, then contact happens at instants without duration.
The scenario involves a sphere and a plane surface; contact is analyzed at successive points.
3
Derive the first contradiction
If the sphere is in contact at two successive instants at two different points without an intervening time, then the sphere would have to be in two places at once or the order of contact would be contradictory.
This is a conditional chain: if no time between contacts, then either multiple contacts simultaneously or a logical paradox.
4
Conclude via reductio
Since the scenario leads to absurdity, the initial assumption (points exist externally) must be false. Therefore, points exist only in the mind as conceptual boundaries of lines.
Al-Razi explicitly states: 'the point has no existence in the external world but only in the mind.'

Source Text

أو ذلك الزمان، وإن لم يتوسط بينهما زمان فقد حصلت الآنات. وأيضًا فالآن الذي هو أول زمان عدم الملاقاة بتلك النقطة إما أن تكون الكرة فيه ملاقية بنقطة أخرى أو لا تكون. فإن كان الأول لم تكن الكرة ملاقية للسطح هناك (خلف). وإن كانت ملاقية فإما أن تكون بينها واسطة فيكون ملاقاة الكرة بتلك الواسطة للسطح قبل ملاقاتها السطح بالنقطة الثانية، فلا تكون ملاقاتها السطح بالنقطة الثانية أول آن حصول عدم الملاقاة بالنقطة الأولى، وقد فرض كذلك (خلف). أو لا تكون بينها واسطة فقد تشاكلت النقط. هذا غاية السلامة في من الحجة وليس فيها عندي نجد إلا أن يقال إنها جارية مع الشك في الدائرة ولم يك. وقد قدمنا أن القول بما لا يستمر مع إثبات الجزء خطأ. القائم على أن الخط المتحرك عليه حتى انتهى إلى آخر لا بد وأن يكون قد عاش بكل فيه كله الخط المتحرك عليه؛ لأن الحركة معا التي بدون نمو شسته غير معقولة. فالخط المتحرك عليه مركب من أمور ما شسته طرف الخط المتحرك طرف الخط المتحرك نقطة، والنقط تتامشه النقطة نقطة، والخط المتحرك عليه مركب من النقاط وهو المطلوب. وهذه الحجة قريبة من التحرير وأجود منها من حيث إنه لا حاجة فيها إلى إثبات الكرة والسطح. لأن المتنازع إن قال: إن الخط المتحرك إنما يلاقي المتحرك عليه بنقطة، فإذا حلقات بعد ذلك نقطة أخرى، لكن الجعون أن يقال: بين تلك النقطتين خط و بين خشك من الزمان. فإن أنكر ذلك فقد صار ثم عن المطلول. أو لا؟ ما تقول: حصول الملاقاة بين النقطتين أشراف آني وحصوله أيضًا آني. لن أقول ذلك الملاقاة معه آلَى. وإذا كان اللاحق آنيًا، فإما أن يكون الذي هو أول زمان حصول عدم الملاقاة بتلك النقطة إما أن تكون خلافًا له أو لا يكون. ويعود التقرير المذكور. الحجة الثانية: والنقطة إن كانت ملاقاة بها (خلف) أو نقطة أخرى ... فإن كانت ملاقية بالنقطة الأولى فيكون الملاقاة بها حاصلة حال حصول... (إلى آخره). ثم قال: وأما النقطة: فإن كانت مستقلة بنفسها وحالة في محل، فإن كان الأول منع الجزء الذي لا ينجزي، وإن كان الثاني كان محلها إما أن يكون مقيًما لازم انفتاحها الانقسام محلها وهو حال أو لا يكون. فإن كان مستقلاً بنفسها فهو المطلوب، والاتحاد في التقسيم. ولا تقطع الاعتدال انتهاء إلى شيء غير منقسم هو المطلوب؛ لأن النقطة لا وجود لها في الخارج بل في الذهن فقط. سلك ذلك. لكن ما المعنى؟ يقول: إنها إما أن تكون في المحاب أو لا تكون. فإن علم بعد الفنية كونها جارية مع المحل سريان الكون في محلة فليس كذلك. إما عنيتم به اجتماع الوجود إلى ما يكون صفة له فهم المحل منا اعتبار. لكن إنما هذا القرد يفيد القاء الانتقام عليها لا ما عن الأول من وجهين: النقطة طرف الخط ونيابته. فإذا كان الخط متناها بالفعل كانت نيابته موجودة بالفعل فكانت النقطة موجودة بالفعل للمحركات المجوسية بداية ونهاية. فالأمر الذي تقع فيه ابتداء وانتهاء لا بد وأن يكون موجودًا بالفعل. فإما أن يكون منقسمًا أو لا يكون، والثاني باطل؛ لأنه لو كان كذلك لكان كل واحد من نصفيه عبد الحركة عامة فيكون الثم الواحد ملاقيًا دفعة واحدة لشيئين وهو محال، أو يكون أحد نصفيه هو المبدا فيكون المبدا من ذلك النصف لا الكل، وقد فرض الحال كذلك (خلف). ثم إذا نقل السلام إلى ذلك النصف فإما أن يكون منقسمًا عاد المحار، وإن لم ينقسم وهو موجود بالفعل. فإن كان مستقلًا بنفسه منع الجزء، وإن افتقر إلى محال عاد القسم. وعن الثاني: أن كون الشيء غنيًا لغيره وصفة له أمر متصور. فلا وليا لا حاجة به إلى التفسير. وإذا كان كذلك فنقوك الموصوف بهذه النقطة إن كان شيئًا غير منقسم هو المطلوب، فإن كان منقسمًا لزم انقسامها لانقسام ذلك الموصوف.
Or that time, and if no time intervenes between them then the instants have occurred. Also, the instant which is the first time of non-contact with that point: either the sphere is then in contact with another point or not. If the former, then the sphere was not in contact with the surface there (contradiction). And if it is in contact, then either there is an intermediary between them, so the sphere's contact with that intermediary with the surface before its contact with the surface at the second point, so its contact with the surface at the second point is not the first instant of occurrence of non-contact with the first point, and it was assumed so (contradiction). Or there is no intermediary between them, then the points coincide. This is the ultimate soundness in the argument, and in my view there is no objection except to say that it proceeds with doubt about the circle and did not... And we have already stated that the statement about what does not persist with the proof of the part is false. The one based on the moving line upon which it moves, until it ends at the last, must have lived in all of it the entire moving line; because motion together which without growth is unintelligible. So the line upon which it moves is composed of things whose growth is the extremity of the moving line, the extremity of the moving line is a point, and points are contiguous point to point, and the line upon which it moves is composed of points, and that is the desired conclusion. This argument is close to the correct one and better than it in that there is no need in it to prove the sphere and surface. Because the disputant, if he says: the moving line only contacts the moving upon it at a point, then when it circles after that another point, but it can be said: between those two points there is a line and between... of time. If he denies that, then he has fallen into the desired conclusion. Or not? What do you say: the occurrence of contact between the two points is an instantaneous occurrence, and its occurrence is also instantaneous. I will not say that contact is with it... And do not cut the middle until it ends at something indivisible, that is the desired conclusion; because the point has no existence in the external world but only in the mind. That is the way. If you meant by it the conjunction of existence to what is a attribute for it, then understanding the locus from us is a consideration. But this monkey only benefits the casting of revenge upon it, not as from the first of two aspects: the point is the extremity and end of the line. So if the line is actual in its finality, then its end is actually existent, so the point is actually existent for the Magian movers as beginning and end. So the thing in which beginning and end occur must be actually existent. So it is either divisible or not; the second is false, because if it were so, then each of its halves would be a slave of motion in general, so that one same thing would meet two things at once, which is impossible, or one of its halves would be the beginning, so the beginning would be from that half not the whole, and it was assumed so (contradiction). Then when the soundness is transferred to that half, it is either divisible, the difficulty returns, or it is not divisible and is actually existent. If it is independent in itself, it precludes the part; and if it depends on a locus, the division recurs. As for the second: that a thing is independent of other and an attribute for it is an intelligible matter. So there is no need for explanation. And if it is so, then our saying: the described by this point, if it is a thing indivisible, that is the desired conclusion; if it is divisible, its division follows from the division of that described.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the actual existence of points in the external world, using arguments from motion and contact. The author, likely al-Razi, presents two main proofs (hujja) by reductio ad absurdum (khulf). The first proof involves a sphere moving and contacting a surface at points. If contact occurs at a point, and then at another point without an intervening time or line, absurdities arise: either the sphere would have two contacts at once or a contradiction in the order of contact. The author argues that these difficulties vanish if points are not actual but merely mental constructs. The second proof directly attacks the notion of a point as an independent entity. If a point is independent and resides in a locus, then either the locus is indivisible (precluding the part) or divisible, leading to regress. If the point is an attribute of a thing, then that thing must be either indivisible (desired conclusion) or divisible, in which case the point divides with it. The author also discusses the point as the extremity of a line: if the line is actual, its extremity (the point) must be actual, but this leads to problems of infinite divisibility. He concludes that points have no existence in the external world (fi al-kharij) but only in the mind (fi al-dhihn). The argument uses conditional logic ('if...then...') throughout, typical of philosophical demonstration in Islamic tradition.

Key Terms
النقطة
الآن
الملاقاة
الخلف
المحل
الانقسام

The Ancients' Proof for Prime Matter: Essence vs. Accident
Author clarifies the ancients' aim and presents the first proof for hyle via divisibility argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical structure of the first ancient proof for hyle and distinguish its purpose from a claim of separate existence.

Core Idea
The ancients' hyle is a conceptual distinction, not a separate substance.

This passage from al-Razi's Mulakkhkhas clarifies the real aim of the ancient philosophers when they introduced prime matter (hayula). It was not to posit a mysterious entity underlying bodies, but to draw attention to the difference between a body's essence and its accidental attributes. Then the first of four proofs is laid out, using a chain of conditional reasoning to argue that hyle must be divisible and extended.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's clarification of the ancients' purpose
Read the opening sentence: 'the purpose is only to draw attention to the difference between the essence of body and its accident.' Note that the ancients are not claiming hyle is a separate entity.
2
Outline the disjunctive starting point
The argument begins: either the hyle (devoid of accidents) is pointed to (mushar ilayha) or not. This is a division into two possibilities.
Being 'pointed to' indicates spatial location.
3
Trace the conditional branching for the first option
If hyle is pointed to, then either it is divisible or indivisible. The text proceeds to examine the indivisible case.
This is a conditional (if-then) step.
4
Analyze the impossibility of an independent point
If hyle is indivisible, it would be a point (nuqta). But a point cannot exist independently because of contradictions involving lines and endpoints, as discussed in the atomism debate.
The argument references a previous discussion (mas'alat al-juz').
5
Conclude the logical outcome
Since a point cannot subsist independently, hyle cannot be indivisible. Therefore, hyle must be divisible, implying it is extended.
This conclusion supports the existence of matter as an extended substrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأظهر أن غرض المتقدمين من هذا الكلام ليس إلا التنبيه على الفرق بين ماهية الجسم وبين عارضها [؟] فالصنوى وهي أربعة: فأن النساء خلق الهيولى عن العودة واحتجوا عليه بوجوه أربعة: فإما أن تكون الهيولى خالية، إما أن تكون مشارًا إليها أو لا تكون. فإن كان الأول، فإما أن تكون قابلة للقسمة أو لا تكون. فإن لم تقبل القسمة كانت نقطة. لكن وجود النقطة على الاستقلال محال، لأنه إذا أتى إليها خطان [؟] فإما أن تجيب بين القطتين اللتين هما طرفا الخطين [؟] على ما مر في مسئلة الجزء أو لا تجيب فالتقطتان داخلتان فيها وهي نائبة عن الخطين فالتقطتان اللتان هما طرف الخطين مبانتان عنها هذا خلاف [؟].
And the more apparent is that the purpose of the ancients from this speech is only to draw attention to the difference between the essence of body and its accident. So the [proofs] are four: Indeed, they [argued] the hyle is void of accident and argued for it with four proofs: either the hyle is empty, either it is pointed to or not. If the first, then either it is divisible or not. If it is not divisible, then it is a point. But the existence of a point independently is impossible, because if two lines come to it, either it lies between the two points that are the endpoints of the lines as passed in the issue of the part, or not. Then the two points are inside it and it stands in for the two lines, so the two points that are the endpoints of the lines are separate from it – this is contrary to the [?].
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is an expansion and explanation by the author (likely al-Razi) of the motive behind the ancient philosophers' introduction of hyle (prime matter). The opening statement clarifies that the ancients did not intend to posit an independent substance separate from body; rather, their aim was merely to highlight the distinction between the essence (mahiyya) of a body and its accidental attributes (awarid). The author then transitions to present the first of the four arguments (wujuh arba'a) that the ancients used to prove the existence of hyle. The argument proceeds by considering whether hyle, if taken as bare substrate devoid of accidents, can be said to be 'pointed to' (mushar ilayha) – i.e., to have spatial location. If it is pointed to, it must be either divisible or indivisible. If indivisible, it would be a point. But a point cannot exist independently, because if two lines end at a point, that point would either coincide with the endpoints or not; both options lead to contradictions discussed earlier in the context of the atomism debate (mas'alat al-juz'). The text here is corrupt, but the logical structure is that a point self-subsisting is impossible, thus hyle cannot be indivisible; it must be divisible, implying extension. This is the classic argument that matter must be extended and divisible. The author's tone is explanatory, not critical, as he is laying out the ancients' reasoning.

Key Terms
الهيولى (al-hayula): Prime matter, the underlying substrate that receives forms and accidents, considered in itself devoid of all determinations. [Note: In later falsafah, hayula is often equated with 'potentiality' (quwwa) as opposed to actuality (fi'l); here it is a metaphysical constituent of bodies.]
المشار إليه (al-mushar ilayhi): Something that can be pointed to, indicating existence in space and time as an individual entity. [Note: Stable term; used in kalam and falsafah to denote concrete particulars.]
النقطة (al-nuqta): A point, conceived as an indivisible location; its independent existence is denied here. [Note: In geometry points are accepted; in physics they are often rejected as independently existing.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) To deny the existence of accidents.
D) To establish that matter is a physical substance separate from form.

Key Takeaway
The ancients' proof for hyle is a conceptual tool to separate essence from accident, not a claim of a new independent substance.

Prime Matter and Quantitative Disposition
Author presents and refutes positions on prime matter's formlessness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical argument on the necessity of prime matter having a quantitative disposition and identify the logical moves used.

Core Idea
Prime matter must have at least a quantitative disposition; otherwise, contradictions arise.

This passage presents a debate on whether prime matter (al-hayula) can be devoid of all forms. The author argues that it cannot be completely formless; it must at least possess a disposition (isti'dad) to receive the bodily form and quantity (miqdar). By carefully following the objections and responses, you will learn how medieval theologians wielded dialectical reasoning to defend their cosmological views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim and objection
The author first states that saying matter is created devoid of body leads to false divisions. An objector (al-Qadir) responds by challenging that the empty matter cannot be like a point because then the argument would entail absurdities about lines and transfers.
Notice that the objector targets the analogy used by the author.
2
Extract the author's refined position
The author clarifies: he does not claim matter is devoid of all forms, only of the bodily form. At that time, matter is described with a disposition (isti'dad) that prepares it to receive body and specific location.
The concept of isti'dad is crucial – it is not a full form but a readiness.
3
Trace the objection about elemental natures
The objector presses that the same reasoning applies to the specification of elemental natures at particular times: if matter has a disposition, why does it receive one form at one time and another later?
This is a common dialectical move: turning the argument back on the opponent.
4
Analyze the final argument from whole and part
The author presents a refined argument: the matter of the whole body and the matter of a part must differ. This difference cannot be in essence or necessary concomitants, so it must be in quantity (miqdar). Therefore, matter cannot be devoid of quantity; otherwise it would be described with quantity while lacking it – a contradiction.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming matter devoid of quantity leads to self-contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد ثبت أن القول بخلق الهيولى عن الجسمية يفضي إلى هذه الأقسام الباطلة. والقادر أن يقول: لم أسلم أن كون الهيولى الخالية كالنقطة، وعند وصول الطول في الخطين قد اتخذتها باشتراط [؟] في الخطين متباينين عن خطيه أقل نصيب منك المتولي حالة في الخط حتى ما علام المحال الذي ألزمتموه، لانتقال المجاز على تلك النقطة أن تنتقل بناتها جانب [؟] ساتر النقط ذلك مجانا، كل واحد من طرفي الخطين أن يصير مباينا عن ذلك الحظ لأن القوات هذا لزم لو اتخذت النقطة تمام ماهيتها وذلك ممنوع. وإن سلمت أفاد هذا القسم علم الجون أن يقال إنه شيء غير مشار إليه، فإذا خلق الله هيولى الجسمية فيها خصصها حين تعين، وقد بينا بطلان ذلك. فالحاصل أنا لا نقول كانت الهيولى خالية عن جميع الصور بل كانت خالية عن الصورة الجسمية إلا أنها كانت عند ذلك موصوفة بما لأجله صارت مستعدة لقبول الجسمية والاختصاص بالجين المعين. سلمنا ذلك لكنه وارد عليكم في اختصاص كل واحد من جريات العناصر بالوقت وفقهم السيرة ذلك انصافه قبل المحصولي ذلك الحين بما لأجله كان اختصاصه بذلك الحين أولى. قلنا: فلم لا يجوز أن تكون الهيولى حال خلوها عن الجسمية موصوفة بما لأجله استعدت لقبول الجسمية والحصول في الحين المعين؟ وعند ذلك يرجع الحال إلى الشوال المذكور قبل أن تجدد العنول عن الصورة إن كان لت أنها وجب استحالة انصفا ما بالصورة وإن كان لذاتها لنا تب فهي ما كانت خالية من الصورة. وقد مر تنظيف هذه الطريقة: هيولى الجسم وهيولى جزء من أجزائه، لو قدرناها مجدين لابد وأن يخلفا، وإلا لكان الشرع غير هو لا مع غيره، وذلك الخلاف لغير الماهية ولا باللوازم، فتعين أن تكون لكون أحدهما كلا والآخر بعضا وذلك هو المقدار، فهيولى حال خلوها عن المقدار موصوفة بالمقدار هذا خلف.
It has been established that the statement that matter is created devoid of body leads to these false divisions. And the Qadir may say: I do not accept that the empty matter is like a point, and when the length reaches in the two lines, I have taken it with the condition [؟] in the two lines, distinct from its two lines, a lesser share of you, the one taking charge of a state in the line, not what is the impossible thing that you necessitated, for the transfer of the metaphor on that point to transfer by its sides [؟] covering the points of that [؟], each one of the two ends of the lines to become distinct from that portion, because this [؟] would be necessary if the point were taken as its complete quiddity, and that is denied. And if it is granted, this section would benefit the knowledge of [؟] that it is said it is something not pointed to, so when Allah creates the matter of body in it, He specifies it at the time of determination, and we have demonstrated the falsehood of that. So the conclusion is that we do not say that matter was devoid of all forms, but rather it was devoid of the bodily form, except that at that time it was described with that due to which it became prepared to receive body and the specification of a particular location. We grant that, but it applies to you regarding the specification of each one of the elemental natures to the time according to their course, that its description before the occurrence of that time with that due to which its specification to that time was more appropriate. We say: why is it not possible that matter, in its state of being devoid of body, is described with that due to which it is prepared to receive body and to occur at the particular time? And then the matter returns to the question mentioned before that the renewal of the accident from the form, if it is because of its essence, then the impossibility of its description with the form is necessary, and if it is because of its essence, then it would not have been devoid of form. And this method has been refined: the matter of the body and the matter of a part of its parts, if we suppose them separated, they must differ; otherwise the share would be not itself without the other, and that difference is not in the essence nor in the necessary concomitants, so it must be because one is the whole and the other a part, and that is quantity; so matter in its state of being devoid of quantity is described with quantity, which is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange regarding the nature of prime matter (hayula) and its relation to form, specifically the bodily form (jism). The author first states that the claim that matter is created devoid of body leads to false divisions. An objector (the Qadir) responds, arguing that he does not accept that empty matter is like a point, and raises difficulties about the transition of the point along lines. The author then clarifies his position: he does not say matter is devoid of all forms, but only of the bodily form; rather, it has a disposition (isti'dad) that prepares it to receive body and to be specified in a particular time. The objector presses that this applies equally to the specification of elemental natures at particular times. The author retorts that it is possible for matter to have such a disposition even when devoid of body. Finally, the author presents a refined argument: if we consider the matter of the whole body and the matter of a part, they must differ in some respect. This difference cannot be in essence or necessary concomitants, so it must be in quantity (miqdar). Therefore, matter cannot be absolutely devoid of quantity, otherwise it would be described with quantity while being devoid of it—a contradiction. The argument uses the concept of necessary difference between whole and part to prove that matter must have some quantitative determination.

الصورة الجسمية

الاستعداد

Analyzing the Dialectical Refutation of Form as Independent Cause
Dialectical refutation of form as independent cause of matter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the dialectical argument that form cannot be the independent cause of matter.

Core Idea
Form's dependence on matter undermines its claim to be an independent cause.

In this passage from al-Mulakhkhas, the author engages in a dialectical (jadal) refutation of the claim that form (sura) is the independent cause of matter (hayula). The argument proceeds by elimination: matter cannot be the cause of form; form cannot be an independent cause; therefore form can only be a partial cause. An objection challenges the initial premise of necessary causal relation. This lesson teaches you to parse such dialectical reasoning and identify points of objection and response.

Guided Path
1
Extract the main thesis under attack
Identify the claim being refuted: 'Form is the independent cause of matter.' This is the mudda'a (claim) that the author seeks to disprove.
2
Identify the premises eliminating matter as cause
Note two reasons: (a) a single thing cannot be both receiver (qabil) and agent (fa'il) simultaneously; (b) matter receives infinite forms, so it cannot cause a specific form.
These reasons are used to reject the possibility that matter causes form.
3
Analyze the argument against form's independence
The author then argues that if form is the cause, it must be either independent or not. Independent is invalid because it leads to multiplicity of causes and effects; non-independent implies form needs matter, making matter prior. Thus form cannot be an absolute cause.
This is a disjunctive syllogism.
4
Locate the opponent's objection
Find where the objector says: 'We do not concede that every two inseparable things must have one as the cause of the other.' This challenges the initial premise of the argument.
The objection also mentions deficiency with relatives, substances, and accidents.
5
Examine the author's response
The author responds by referring to a previous clarification about matter's uniform relation to forms, but acknowledges obstacles. He ends by alluding to the discussion of elements.
The response is incomplete; the debate continues beyond this excerpt.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على هذه المقدمات قالوا لما ثبت لائحة الهيولى والصورة وثبت أن كل ما كان كذلك فلا بد أن يكون أحدهما علة للآخر إما أن يكون الهيولى علة لوجود الصورة أو الصورة علة للهيولى الأول باطل لوجهين أما أولا فلأن الشيء الواحد لا يكون بالذات قابلا وفاعلا معا وأما ثانيا فلأن المادة قابلة لصور لا نهاية لها فلا تكون علة لصورة واحدة فبقي كون الصورة علة فلا يخلو إما أن تكون علة مستقلة وذلك باطل لأن العلة الناقلة تستلزم تعدد العلل والمعلولات ولا يصح مع علة واحدة مطلقة وإن لم تكن مستقلة فهي محتاجة في ذاتها إلى الهيولى وكل ما كان كذلك كان محتاجا في فاعليته فيكون تأثيرها في وجود الهيولى بمشاركة من الهيولى فيكون الهيولى سابقا على تأثيرها والشكل موجود مع المادة فالمادة متقدمة على الشكل فالمادة متقدمة على الصورة فيستحيل كون الصورة علة مطلقة إلا أن تكون الصورة شريكة لغيرها وذلك إما أن يكون جسما حارا أو مجردا وتحقيق ذلك أن المجرد يكون علة لوجود الهيولى لما استحال انفكاك الهيولى عن الصورة فاستبقاء حقيقة الهيولى إلى استحضار الصورة لما يخصها أو نوعيا هذا خلاصة كلامهم ثم الاعتراض لا نسلم أن كل متلازمين لا بد أن يكون أحدهما علة للآخر وما الدلالة عليه وبعد فراغكم عن العلة فهي منقوصة بالمضافين وبالجواهر والأعراض سلمتم ذلك لكن لا يمكن كون الهيولى علة لقوله لأن الواحد لا يكون قابلا وفاعلا معا قلت قد سبق تنقية مقالة نسبة المادة إلى جميغ الصور واحدة قلنا هذا لا يستمر على تولد موانع لإخلال وأما العناصر فذلك محل كلام
The discourse on these premises: They said: since the hylomorphic composition [of matter and form] is established, and it is established that whenever this is the case then necessarily one of them is the cause of the other. Either matter is the cause of the existence of form, or form is the cause of matter. The first is invalid for two reasons: first because a single thing cannot be both a receiver and an agent simultaneously; second because matter is receptive to infinite forms, so it cannot be the cause of a single form. Thus what remains is that form is the cause. Then it is either an independent cause—and that is invalid because the causal principle entails multiplicity of causes and effects and is not valid with a single absolute cause. Or it is not independent—then it is in itself in need of matter, and whatever is like that is in need of its activity, so its effect in the existence of matter would be through the participation of matter, thus matter is prior to its effect. And form exists together with matter, so matter is prior to form, hence matter is prior to form, so it is impossible for form to be an absolute cause, unless form is a partner of something else, whether it be a hot body or a separate intellect. The verification of that is that the separate entity is a cause for the existence of matter because it is impossible for matter to be free of form, so the preservation of matter's essence requires the presence of form that is specific to it or generic. This is the summary of their statement. Then the objection: We do not concede that every two mutually inseparable things must have one as the cause of the other. What is the evidence for that? And after your completion of the causality, it is deficient with respect to relatives and substances and accidents. You conceded that, but it is not possible for matter to be the cause because the one cannot be both receiver and agent. I say: The purification of the claim that the relationship of matter to all forms is one has already preceded. We say: This does not hold due to the generation of obstacles that cause disruption. As for the elements, that is the locus of discussion.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage presents a critical dialectical argument concerning the relationship between matter (hayula) and form (sura) in Aristotelian physics. The author begins by stating a premise: since matter and form are always found together, one must be the cause of the other. Then he dismisses the possibility that matter causes form, giving two reasons. First, a single thing cannot simultaneously be a receiver (qabil) and an agent (fa'il)—matter is a receiver, not an agent. Second, matter can receive many forms, so it cannot be the cause of a specific form. Consequently, the only remaining candidate is that form is the cause of matter. However, the author then examines whether form can be an independent cause. He argues that if form were independent, it would cause multiple effects, but a single absolute cause cannot produce multiple effects without mediation. Moreover, form needs matter for its existence—a form cannot exist without matter—so form is dependent on matter. Therefore form cannot be an absolute cause; if it is a cause, it must be a partial cause (mushtarik). The author then considers two possibilities: either form is a body (like a hot body imparting heat) or a separate intellect. The separate intellect could be a cause for matter because it sustains the form that matter requires. The passage then records an objection: the opponent denies the initial premise that two inseparable things must have one as the cause of the other. The opponent also points out that the argument about causality is flawed when applied to relatives, substances, and accidents. The objection concedes that matter cannot be a cause because it cannot be both receiver and agent. The author replies by previously clarifying that matter's relation to all forms is uniform, but this does not hold due to generated obstacles. Finally, the author alludes to discussion of elements (anäsir) as a further locus of the debate, ending the excerpt. The style is typical of philosophical kalam: stating a position, refuting alternatives, raising objections, and responding. All examples used (hot body, separate intellect, elements) are from natural philosophy, not logic per se, but the argumentative structure is dialectical.

Key Takeaway
The dialectical refutation shows that form's dependence on matter prevents it from being an independent cause, but the opposition's challenge to the initial premise remains unresolved in this excerpt.

Refuting Substantial Forms with Dialectical Argument
Dialectical refutation of celestial substantial forms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Razi's dialectical refutation of substantial forms for celestial bodies and explain the infinite regress objection.

Core Idea
If differences require forms, those forms require prior forms ad infinitum.

In this passage from the Mulakhkhas, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi engages in a dialectical demolition of the Aristotelian claim that celestial bodies need distinct substantial forms. He systematically presents objections and responses, culminating in a powerful infinite regress argument. This lesson will guide you through the structure of his refutation so you can recognize and deploy such arguments in your own intellectual engagements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's thesis
The opponent holds that celestial bodies require specific substantial forms to account for their unique attributes (e.g., each sphere's unchanging motion). Find where this claim is stated or implied in the passage.
Look for references to 'صور نوعية' (specific forms) for celestial bodies.
2
List the author's objections
Al-Razi raises several objections before the main infinite regress. For example, the objection that shape (shakl) is not inseparable from body, and the objection from the One (emanation). Note each objection and the response.
These serve to undermine the opponent's presuppositions.
3
Focus on the infinite regress argument
The core argument: if bodies differ in attributes, and those attributes require specific forms, then those forms themselves must differ, requiring further forms, ad infinitum. Locate where al-Razi presents this and the opponent's attempted response.
The opponent tries to stop the regress by appealing to different matters for celestial spheres.
4
Analyse the counter-counterargument
Al-Razi counters by applying the same logic to qualities: if differences in qualities can be explained without forms, then the need for forms is obviated. Find where he makes this point.
This shows the opponent's defense is arbitrary.
5
Evaluate the final move
Al-Razi even questions whether the inability of celestial spheres to accept multiple qualities requires a permanent form at all. Identify this concluding objection.
The dialectic ends with the burden back on the opponent.

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of the argument that celestial bodies require substantial forms different from their body-ness to account for their specific attributes. The author poses several objections and responds to each. The first objection: if generation and corruption are admitted, why isn't form an absolute cause? The answer: body itself is the cause. The second: dependence on a prior cause. The author rejects that this leads to a regress. The third: the claim that body is always with shape. The author argues that shape is subsequent to magnitude, so shape cannot be concomitant with body-ness. The fourth: the argument from the One (the emanation theory) is rejected. Then the author turns to the main point: all bodies share body-ness, so if differences require specific forms, those forms would require further forms ad infinitum. The opponent tries to avoid regress by appealing to prior forms in elemental bodies and differences in celestial matter. The author counters by applying the same reasoning to qualities, showing that the need for substantial forms is obviated if we substitute qualities. Finally, the author questions the necessity of positing a specific form as the cause of the celestial sphere's inability to accept multiple qualities, arguing that the cause could be something else. Throughout, the argument is dialectical, with each objection met by a response that undermines the need for substantial forms.

Critique of Substantial Forms in Celestial Bodies
Author argues against the necessity of substantial forms for celestial bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument against the necessity of substantial forms for celestial bodies and identify the two main objections (regress and circularity).

Core Idea
Substantial forms are not logically necessary for celestial bodies; matter alone can explain qualitative differences.

The author challenges the Peripatetic claim that celestial bodies require specific substantial forms as principles of their qualities. This critique uses logical reasoning to show that the arguments for forms lead to regress or circularity, and that alternative explanations based on matter alone are sufficient. Understanding this critique sharpens your ability to assess arguments about causality in Islamic natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
Find the claim being critiqued: that celestial bodies have specific substantial forms (suwar naw'iyya) which are the principles of their different qualities (like shape, motion, etc.).
The opponent holds that differences in accidents require different forms.
2
Follow the regress argument
The author argues that if different qualities require different forms, then the forms themselves, being different, would require further forms to explain their differences, leading to an infinite regress (tatbiq).
Key phrase: 'wa yalzim al-tatbiq' (a regress follows).
3
Understand the opponent's distinction
The opponent distinguishes elemental bodies (which have prior forms in matter) from celestial bodies (which have fixed forms due to different quiddities). This is meant to stop the regress.
The opponent claims each sphere has a unique quiddity that receives its own form.
4
See the author's counter
The author shows that the same reasoning can be applied to qualities themselves: elemental bodies receive qualities through prior qualities, and celestial bodies simply lack receptivity to other qualities. Thus no forms are needed.
The point is that material dispositions alone can explain the differences without invoking forms.
5
Identify the circularity argument
The author argues that if forms are causes of corporeality, then since forms themselves depend on corporeality for existence, a circularity (dawr) results. This undermines the claim that forms are the principles of bodies.
The author also notes that even if a single form produces multiple effects, that does not prove it is a substantial form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعضها لا يقبل الشكل الواحد وهو الفلك. وقد قيل: إن اختلاف الأعراض يكون لاختلاف صور نوعية موجودة في ملكة الجرمو؟ والاعتراض لأن اشتراك الأجسام بأسرها في الجسمية سلمنا ذلك لكن الأجسام كما اختلفت في الصفات التي نعنيها فقد اختلفت أيضًا في الصور التي جعلتموها مبادئ تلك الصفات، فلو كان اختصاصها بتلك الصفات يجب أن يكون بصور نوعية لكان اختصاصها بتلك الصور يجب أن يكون بصور أخرى ثم الكلام فيها كالكلام في الأول ويلزم التبليك. لا يقال: اختصاص الجسم العنصري المعين بالصورة المعينة إنما كان لأن المادة قبل حدوث تلك الصورة فيها كانت موصوفة بصورة أخرى لأجلها استعدت المادة لقبول الصورة اللاحقة. وأما اختصاص الأجسام الفلكية بصورها النوعية فلكل فلك مخالفة بالماهية للمادة الفلك الآخر وكل واحدة تقبل الصورة التي حملت فيها لأنا نقول: إذا جوزتم ذلك في [؟] فجوزوا مثله في الكيفيات حتى نقول: الأجسام العنصرية إنما اختلف كل واحد منها بالكيفية المعينة لأنه كان قبل الاتصاف بتلك الكيفية موصوفًا بكيفية أخرى لأجلها استعدت المادة لقبول الكيفية اللاحقة. وأما الأجسام الفلكية فإنما اختص كل واحد منها بكيفيته المعينة لأن الفلك لا يقبل لكيفية أخرى. وعلى هذا التقدير تسقط الحاجة إلى إثبات هذه الصورة. سلمنا أنه لا بد من أمر ذاتي في الفلك ليكون مبدأ لهذه الكيفيات المختلفة، لكن لم علم أنه لا بد من إثبات ذلك؟ فإن كونه بحيث لا يقبل الفلك لكيفيات مختلفة لا يمكن القطع بأنه لا بد وأن يكون لأجل صورة دارية؟ لأن تلك الصورة إن لم تكن للماهية الجسمية لذلك الفلك لم يكن الحكم المعطى وهو عدم قبول الكيفيات المختلفة لأنها وإن كانت لائحة فذلك الكرم ليس للنفس الجسمية ولا لشيء من لوازمها ولا لما لا يكون حالاً فيها ولا محلاً لها لأن كل ذلك باطل. وبتقدير صحته فالمقصود حاصل لأنه لو جاز أن يكون لزوم تلك الصور لأجل هذا القسم فليجز مثل لزوم الشكل والمقدار. فتعين أن كون ذلك اللزوم للمادة. وإذا كانت المادة كافية في لزوم تلك الصورة فلم لا تكفي في لزوم هذه الأعراض من غير حاجة إلى إثبات هذه الصور. وأما العناصر فمنها ما يقبل الأشكال بسهولة فهو الرطب ومنها ما يقبلها بعسر وهو اليابس. إذا كان كذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن سهولة قبول المشكلات معللة بعلة وجودية، وأما صعوبة قبولها فأنها معللة بعلة عدمية وهي عدم تلك العلة، أو بالعكس. وعلى هذا التقدير لا يظهر انتفاع خلق الأجسام عن الصورة النوعية. مع أنه لا بد للصفات الثلث من إسنادها إلى علم وجودية. فلم قيل إن تلك الصور النوعية؟ بيان أن الصورة عبارة عن الحال المقوم لمحله إلى الحال الذي يكون سببا لوجود محله. وإذا عرفت ذلك فإن الحجة المذكورة دالة على تعليل هذه الأعراض بأمور موجودة في الأجسام لكن لا بد من الدلالة على أن تلك الأمور أسباب لوجود تلك الأجسام حتى يكون تلك الأمور صورًا وإلا فلا تكون صورًا أبدًا. وإلى الآن ما رأينا أحدًا منهم حاول إثبات هذا المطلوب. سلمنا أن الحجة التي ذكروها تنتج إثبات الصور النوعية لكن همنا ما يدل على نفيها من وجهين: هذه الصور إما أن تكون حالة في الجسم أو في الهيولى لكن بشرط حلول الجسم فيها. ومع التقديرين فاتقان وجودها محتاجة إلى الجسمية. فلو كانت الجسمية معللة بها لزم الدور. إلا أن يقول في الجسم صورة واحدة هي عِدَةُ ما فيه من الكيف والمقدار والشكل. فإنهم قد حكموا بأنه قد صدر عن الصورة الواحدة أكثر من أثر واحد وهو عندهم جائز.
And some of them do not accept a single shape, and that is the celestial sphere. And it was said: The difference of accidents is due to the difference of specific forms existing in the substance of the body? The objection is because the sharing of all bodies in corporeality—we grant that, but just as bodies differ in the qualities that we mean, they also differ in the forms that you make the principles of those qualities. So if their particularization by those qualities must be by specific forms, then their particularization by those forms must be by other forms, and then the discussion about them is like the discussion about the first, and a regress follows. It cannot be said: The particularization of a specific elemental body by a specific form is only because matter before the occurrence of that form was described by another form, for which matter became prepared to receive the subsequent form. As for the particularization of celestial bodies by their specific forms, it is because each sphere has a different quiddity from the matter of the other sphere, and each one receives the form that it contains. For we say: If you allow that, then allow the like in qualities until we say: Elemental bodies differ from one another by a specific quality because before being described with that quality, it was described with another quality for which matter became prepared to receive the subsequent quality. As for celestial bodies, they are each particularized by their specific quality because the sphere does not accept another quality. And on this estimation, the need for proving these forms falls away. We grant that there must be an essential thing in the sphere to be the principle of these different qualities, but how do we know that it must be proved? For its being such that the sphere does not accept different qualities cannot be decisively proven that it must be due to a form? Because if that form is not for the corporeal essence of that sphere, then the given judgment—which is the non-acceptance of different qualities—[would not hold] because even if they are evident, that judgment is not for the soul itself, nor for any of its necessary concomitants, nor for something that is neither a state in it nor a substrate for it, because all that is false. And assuming its correctness, the purpose is achieved because if it is permissible that the entailment of those forms is due to this division, then let the like be permissible for the entailment of shape and magnitude. So it is determined that the entailment belongs to matter. And if matter is sufficient for the entailment of that form, why is it not sufficient for the entailment of these accidents without the need to prove these forms? As for the elements, some of them accept shapes easily—that is the moist—and some accept them with difficulty—that is the dry. Since this is the case, why is it not permissible to say that the ease of accepting shapes is explained by an existent cause, and the difficulty of accepting them is explained by a non-existent cause—namely, the absence of that cause—or the opposite? And on this estimation, the benefit of creating bodies from the specific form does not appear. And there is no escape from attributing the three qualities to an existing cause. So why is it said that those specific forms? The explanation is that the form is the state that constitutes its substrate to the state that is a cause for the existence of its substrate. And when you know that, the mentioned argument indicates explaining these accidents by things existing in bodies, but it is necessary to indicate that those things are causes for the existence of those bodies so that those things are forms; otherwise they are never forms. Up to now we have not seen anyone among them who attempted to prove this claim. We grant that the argument they mentioned yields proof of specific forms, but what we care about indicates their negation from two aspects: these forms are either a state in the body or in the hyle but with the condition of the body's being in it. And in both estimations, their existence depends on corporeality. So if corporeality is explained by them, a circularity follows. Unless one says that in the body there is a single form that is the sum of what is in it of quality, magnitude, and shape. For they have ruled that more than one effect may issue from a single form, and that is permissible in their view.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the falsafa claim that celestial bodies have specific substantial forms (suwar naw'iyya) that are the principles of their different qualities (such as shape, motion, and other accidents). The author presents a dialectical exchange: an opponent argues that differences in accidents require different forms, and the author refutes this by showing that material dispositions alone could account for the differences, and that the arguments for forms lead to infinite regress or circularity. The author first notes that if bodies sharing corporeality could have different qualities due to forms, then those forms themselves would require further forms to explain their differences, leading to an infinite regress. The opponent tries to avoid this by distinguishing between elemental bodies (which have a history of successive forms) and celestial bodies (which have fixed forms due to different quiddities). The author counters that the same reasoning can be applied to qualities themselves—elemental bodies receive qualities through prior qualities, while celestial bodies simply lack the receptivity to other qualities, so no form is needed. The author further argues that even if a principle is needed, it could be the matter itself, not a form. The author then discusses the elements (moist and dry) and suggests that differences in receptivity can be explained by presence or absence of a cause, not necessarily a form. Finally, the author criticizes the notion that forms are causes for the existence of bodies, pointing out that this leads to a circularity if corporeality is explained by forms. The overall critique is aimed at the logical necessity of postulating such forms.

Key Terms
صورة نوعية (sūra nawʿiyya)
جسم فلكي (jism falakī)
مادة (mādda)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because each body must have an infinite number of forms.
B) Because if forms explain qualities, then the differences between forms themselves would require further forms.
C) Because celestial bodies cannot have forms.
D) Because forms are not real.

Key Takeaway
The author argues that positing substantial forms to explain qualitative differences leads to an infinite regress, and that material dispositions can suffice, undermining the necessity of such forms.

The Sphere as the Only Simple Shape
Author argues that sphere is the only shape for a simple body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definitional structure of a simple body's shape and explain why the sphere must be its shape.

Core Idea
Simple shape means uniform nature and single action; only sphere satisfies.

In this passage, the author presents a definitional argument that establishes the sphere as the only shape appropriate for a simple body. This reasoning is foundational in Islamic cosmology, linking the metaphysical principle of simplicity to the physical shape of celestial spheres. By parsing this argument, you will learn how a hadd haqiqi (real definition) is constructed using genus and differentia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'شكل البسيط' (the shape of the simple body). Find where the author states what is being defined.
The conclusion 'فاذن شكل البسيط الكرة' confirms the definiendum.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is the broader category: a shape. The differentia is what distinguishes this shape: having one nature (طبيعة واحدة) and producing one action (فعل واحد).
The text states 'له طبيعة واحدة' and 'لا تفعل المادة الواحدة إلا فعلاً واحداً'.
3
Understand the exclusionary argument
The author shows that any shape other than the sphere would have multiple actions (different sides: line, radius, point), which contradicts the single nature of a simple body.
This elimination step is crucial for the definition.
4
Formulate the complete definition
Combine genus and differentia: The simple shape is a sphere (genus: shape, differentia: having one nature and one action).
This is a hadd haqiqi (real definition) because it captures the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشكل البسيط له طبيعة واحدة وهي لا تفعل المادة الواحدة إلا فعلاً واحداً وكل شكل سوى الكرة ففيه أفعال مختلفة فانه يكون جانب منه خطا وآخر ناوياً وآخر نقطة. فاذن شكل البسيط الكرة
The simple shape has one nature, and a single matter does only one action. Every shape other than the sphere has different actions, for one side of it is a line, another a radius, and another a point. Therefore the shape of the simple [body] is the sphere.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a definitional argument establishing the sphere as the only shape appropriate for a simple body (such as the celestial sphere). The reasoning follows: a simple body has a single nature, and a single nature produces a single action. Any shape other than the sphere would require multiple actions—for example, a cube has flat faces that would require different motions relative to the center. Thus, only the sphere, with its uniform curvature, allows a single action. This is a foundational argument in Aristotelian cosmology, linking simplicity of nature to spherical shape.

Key Terms
شكل بسيط (shakl basit: simple shape)
طبيعة واحدة (tabi'a wahida: single nature)
فعل واحد (fi'l wahid: single action)
الكرة (al-kura: the sphere)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is the most beautiful shape.
B) Because only the sphere allows a single action from a single nature.
C) Because it is the only shape that can be divided equally.
D) Because it is the shape of the Earth.

Key Takeaway
The sphere is the only shape that can correspond to a simple body because only it allows a single action from a single nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Definition of the Simple Body
Author defines the simple body as not composed of different natures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the simple body and distinguish between its two types based on the definition.

Core Idea
The simple body is not simple by indivisibility, but by lack of composition from different natures.

In this passage, the author provides a clear definition of the simple body (al-jirm al-basit) and then classifies it into two types. This definition is foundational for understanding Islamic cosmology and physics, where the basic building blocks of the universe are either celestial or elemental. Let's unpack the definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the Arabic phrase that names the definiendum: 'al-jirm al-basit'. Note that it is a 'body' (jirm) with a qualification 'simple' (basit).
2
Extract the defining condition
The author states: 'لا يكون حقيقته مركبة من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع' – its reality is not composed of bodies of different natures. This is the core of the definition: it excludes composition from diverse natures.
The term 'طبيعة' (nature) here refers to the intrinsic property of a body (e.g., hotness, coldness, wetness, dryness).
3
Identify the two categories
After defining, the author divides the simple body into two types: celestial (فلكية) and elemental (عنصرية). Understand what each category represents.
Celestial bodies are the spheres and stars; elemental bodies are the pure elements (fire, air, water, earth) themselves.
4
Test the definition with examples
Apply the definition: Is a rock a simple body? No, because a rock is a mixture of elements. Is the moon? Yes, because it is a celestial body composed of a single celestial nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبقى رادياً بالبيطرة ههنا الجرم الذي لا يكون حقيقته مركبة من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع فهي إما فلكية أو عنصرية
And what is meant by 'simple body' here is a body whose reality is not composed of bodies of different natures. So it is either celestial or elemental.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage provides a definition (rasm) of the simple body (al-jirm al-basit). It clarifies that 'simple' does not mean absolutely indivisible, but rather that its essence is not a composite of bodies with differing natures (like the four elements). The simple body is then divided into two categories: celestial (falakiyya), referring to the heavens, and elemental ('unsuriyya), referring to the basic elements themselves (fire, air, water, earth) in their pure form. This definition is crucial for understanding the physical cosmology of the Mulakhkhas.

Key Terms

فلكية
عنصرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The simple body is defined not by absolute indivisibility, but by the absence of composition from bodies with differing natures, and it is divided into celestial and elemental types.

Definition of Simple Body and Its Division
Author defines simple body and divides it into celestial and elemental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a simple body (al-jism al-basit) and distinguish it from a composite body using the criteria of reality (haqiqa) and sense (hiss).

Core Idea
A simple body is not a single particle but a body whose every part shares the same nature, unlike a composite made of different natures.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi explains what it means for a body to be 'simple' (basit). He distinguishes between reality and sense, and between celestial and elemental bodies. This is a foundational distinction in Islamic natural philosophy that every student must grasp to understand larger discussions about matter and form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key concepts: simple and composite
Find where the author defines simple body and composite body. Simple means parts share the same name (e.g., any part of water is water). Composite means made from bodies of different natures (e.g., bone and flesh).
2
Distinguish the two perspectives: reality (haqiqa) and sense (hiss)
In reality, items like bone and flesh are considered simple relative to the four elements. In sense, they appear as two distinct simples. The celestial sphere is not simple in sense because what we sense of it is not the sphere itself.
3
Determine the final definition of simple body in this context
After examining perspectives, the author settles: simple body is a body whose reality is not composed of bodies of different natures. This includes both celestial and elemental bodies.
4
Identify the division: celestial and elemental
The passage concludes by dividing simple bodies into celestial (falak) and elemental (elemental bodies like fire, air, water, earth).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو مبني على مقدمة وقعيد وخاتمة. ان المقدمة مع حقيقة البسط والمركب الجسم البسيط قد يرسم مع نفي الهيولى جانه الذي يكون جزوه مساويا لكل اسم واحد وجانه الذي حقيقته من اجسام مختلفة الطبائع. فإذا اعتبرنا الأول لحب الحقيقة فإليكن العظم واللحم وما يشبهما بسطا انتهائية من العناصر الأربعة. وإذ العتبرناه لحب الحس كانا بسيطين لكن الفلك لايكون بسطا لأن المحسوس منه ليس بفلك. واما الثاني فاعتباره لحب الحقيقة يمنع من بساطة الحج والعظم واللحم ويمنع من بساطة الفلك. ومن عرفت البسط عرفت الذي يقابله. وبقى المراد بالبسط ههنا الجرم الذي لا يكون حقيقته مركبة من اجسام مختلفة الطبائع فهي إما فلكية العنصرية القسم الأول في الفلكيات.
It is built on a premise, a middle, and a conclusion. The premise: regarding the nature of the simple and the composite, the simple body is sometimes defined with the negation of matter, its part being equal to the whole in name, and the composite whose reality is from bodies of different natures. If we consider the first in reality, then bone, flesh, and what resembles them are simple elements from the four elements. And if we consider it in sense, they are two simples, but the celestial sphere is not simple because what is sensed from it is not a sphere. As for the second, considering it in reality prevents the simplicity of stone, bone, and flesh, and prevents the simplicity of the celestial sphere. Whoever knows the simple knows its opposite. What remains meant by simple here is the body whose reality is not composed of bodies of different natures; it is either celestial or elemental. The first section is on celestial bodies.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'simple body' (al-jism al-basit) and distinguishes it from composite. The author begins by stating that the discussion is built on a structure of premise, middle, and conclusion. The premise explains that a simple body is defined without matter, meaning its parts are homonymous with the whole (e.g., any part of water is still water). A composite body, in contrast, has parts of different natures (e.g., bone and flesh). Then two perspectives are considered: reality (haqiqa) and sense (hiss). In reality, items like bone and flesh are considered simple relative to the four elements. In sense, they are two distinct simples. However, the celestial sphere (falak) is not simple in the sensed aspect because what is sensed of it is not the sphere itself. Considering the second perspective (the composite) in reality negates the simplicity of stone, bone, flesh, and also the celestial sphere. Thus, the intended meaning of 'simple' in this context is any body whose reality is not composed of bodies of different natures. This divides into celestial and elemental bodies. The passage then transitions to the first section concerning celestial bodies.

Key Terms
جسم بسيط
مركب
فلك

D) A body that is sensed as a single entity.

Key Takeaway
A simple body is defined by the homogeneity of its parts, not by absolute indivisibility, and it is divided into celestial and elemental bodies.

Al-Razi's Critique of Celestial Motion Arguments
Author critiques arguments for planetary motions and defends the traditional count of nine spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key points of al-Razi's critique of the argument that planetary motion requires a moving sphere, and evaluate the epistemological status of the premise that celestial motions are unchanging.

Core Idea
Al-Razi shows that apparent harmony does not prove a single moving sphere, and the number of spheres is uncertain.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi was a master of critical thinking, never accepting premises without rigorous examination. In this passage from the Mulakhkhas, he turns his analytical eye on Ptolemaic astronomy, questioning the necessity of moving spheres and the certainty of the traditional nine-sphere cosmos. His critique forces us to distinguish between empirical observation and demonstrative proof—a distinction at the heart of Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
What is the argument that al-Razi is critiquing? Look for the claim that planets cannot move independently and must be carried by spheres. He begins by stating the counterargument: if planets moved by themselves, their motions would not be coordinated.
The target claim is that the harmony of planetary motions proves the existence of a single moving sphere for each planet.
2
Extract al-Razi's objections
Al-Razi offers several objections. First, the opponent suggests that a transparent ring could carry the planet without moving the sphere. Second, he questions the inference from motion similarity to a single agent. Third, he raises the possibility of multiple spheres beyond the ninth. List these objections in order.
Pay attention to how each objection challenges a different premise of the original argument.
3
Analyze the epistemological critique
Al-Razi argues that the ancients observed apparent order and assumed it was unchanging, but this is not a demonstrative premise. He points out that later scholars mistakenly treated it as a proof. Identify the epistemological error: treating empirical observation as certain without verifying the premises.
This step requires you to understand the difference between certainty (yaqin) and mere appearance (zann).
4
Assess al-Razi's alternative
Al-Razi does not provide a definitive alternative but shows that other possibilities are conceivable, such as a transparent ring or additional spheres. The conclusion is that the traditional view is not proved. How does this critique strengthen the case for skepticism in astronomical claims?
Al-Razi's approach is to undermine certainty, not to replace the theory.
