وهذا الاحتمال واقع لان الذي يمكن ان يتمك بمع الحد فكرة النواب اين ١٨٤ الاان مكاننا مائة وحتى كان كنند كانت مركونة فكرة واحدة والمقدمات غير نفينتين اما الصغرى فلات حركاتها وان كانت في الحر منشابية لكن العلما لايكون في الحقيقة واحدة لا فالوقتد ناات الواحدة منها تم التودة فست وثلثين الف سنة والاخرى تتم هلع هذا الالتحان لكن نقصان عاشرة واحدة او اقل فالذي يخص الدرجة الواحة من هذا الصدد من التفاوت يقل جدا بحيث انني اعادنا بضبطه وإذا اجمل ذلك سقط القطع بالتشابه واما الكبرى ولاها و اشتراك الاشية الة لفترة لازم واحد و اعلم ان هذه الاتصالات غير مختصة بكرة الثوابت بل هو حاصل ف كل الكرات والذي يقال في إبطاله من ان اقرب قرب كك كوكب يساوى ابعد بعد كل الكواكب التي فرضت تحته فقد عرفت ضعف من هذا الباب بل يحبون لن تكون فوق الفلك التاسع المتحرك بالحركة اليومية من الافلاك مالانعا عندها الا الله تعالى بر عمل ان يكون هذا الفلك الناسخ بما فيه من الكرات ماكو ناغ نر كرة اخرى عظمة ويكون من نحن تلك الفلك الكرة الف الفكرة مثل هذه الكرات كلية اك مبتعد لغات تدوير المريخ اعظم من مثل الشمس فاذا عقل ذلك فاتى باس بان يفرض مثله مما هو ا عظم منه في أن الحركات التاويلة لا يع من ها الإحالة الاظهرات القدماء لم أولو الحركات السماوية وجد والاختلافات العارضة لها عادلة وظاهر واحد قوى في ظنونم انتا في الفها غير مختلفة أصلا الفلوق عليه هذا الاختلاف لوجدوا ولما اختص نظامها بوجه واحد ولما اسمات مشاهد تم لذلك النظام حکموا بانها لا تختلف اصلا مادامت باقية فبنوا ع ا هذا الأصل مبا حثم النجومية و الماخرون ظنواكون هذه المقدمة برهانية والذي يمكن نصرفا به من أصولهم.
And as for [the claim] that the motion of the sphere is necessary for the motion of the planets, if not for that, their motions would be ahead or behind each other, because it is unlikely that the measure of the [planetary] speeds and periods with the difference of their [orbital] degrees would be preserved [leading to] the preservation of the positions of some relative to others, to the extent that their motion resembles the motion of a single sphere around two poles and an equator. And that is strengthened by their agreement in longitudinal motions, meaning that each one has a specific amount of years for a specific motion relative to the equinoctial point. And what is specific to the planets is that a single planet traverses from its [orbit] to two different groups [of stars]. If the planets moved by themselves, [then] the existence of the required [order] would be impossible. [The opponent] says: [You have argued that] there is no proof for the impossibility of [independent] motion on the spheres. [We] grant that, but why cannot it be said that a ring-like body separates from the sphere and is suspended, and its diameter equals the diameter of the planet, and when the planet moves, that ring moves along with it, and the sphere remains stationary without being affected? Moreover, we do not observe the motion of that ring because it is transparent and similar to the body of the firmament. On this assumption, the independence of the planet in motion does not necessitate the motion of the sphere. And there are other refutations: [third objection] that [the planet] moves multiple [objects] at once, and [fourth] that it does not move all the planets. Then the second objection regarding the number of spheres: when people believed that the planet does not move except by the motion of the sphere, they inferred from the varieties of motions the number of spheres. And the well-known [view] is that the celestial spheres are nine. I think that the opposite is not known, and just as its [the number's] knowledge is not known, its [refutation] is not known either. For this reason, the Shaykh did not decide whether the sphere of the fixed stars is one sphere or multiple spheres nested within each other. This possibility is real, because that which can be conceived [in this regard] is that the motion of the fixed stars is one motion, but the premises are not certain. As for the minor premise, [the claim] that their motions, though similar in appearance, are not in reality one, because the period of one of them completes in 36,000 years, and the other completes in the same period but with a deficit of one tenth or less, so the difference per degree is very small such that we cannot perceive it. If that is taken together, the certainty of similarity is lost. As for the major premise, [the claim] that shared [motions] require a single agent [is also uncertain]. Know that these [shared] connections are not specific to the sphere of fixed stars but occur in all spheres. And what is said to refute it, namely that the nearest approach of each planet equals the farthest distance of the planets below it, you already know the weakness of this approach. Rather, it is possible that above the ninth sphere, which moves with the daily motion, there are spheres whose count is known only to God. It can be that this encompassing sphere, with its included spheres, is like an immense sphere, and within that sphere [there exist] another thousand spheres like these entire spheres? The epicycle of Mars is larger than the Sun. If that is conceived, there is no impossibility in positing something greater than it, and the [diversity of] motions does not prevent that. The ancients, when they observed celestial motions and their variations, saw one strong apparent order and thought that they are essentially unchanging. If they had found variation, they would have found that their system is not limited to one aspect, and because they witnessed that system, they judged that they do not change as long as they remain. So they built their astronomical inquiries on this principle. The later [scholars] thought that this premise is demonstrative. And the way to refute it from their own principles is [as follows].
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of arguments about celestial motions and the number of celestial spheres. The author (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) engages with the claim that planets do not move independently but by the motion of their respective spheres. He presents a series of objections and counterarguments. First, he states the problem: if planets moved independently, their motions would not be harmonized, yet they appear coordinated like a single sphere around poles and an equator. This suggests a single motion. However, an opponent argues that this does not prove that the sphere moves; perhaps a transparent ring attached to the planet moves with it, leaving the sphere stationary. The author acknowledges this possibility and notes that we do not observe such a ring because it is transparent. Next, he discusses the inference from the number of distinct motions to the number of spheres. The ancients believed there are nine spheres because they thought each planet moves only with its sphere. But the author questions the certainty of this inference: the motions of the fixed stars appear similar but their periods differ slightly (one completing in 36,000 years, another with a slight deficit), so we cannot be certain they are identical. Moreover, the principle that shared motions require a single agent is not specific to the fixed stars but applies to all spheres. The common argument that the nearest approach of one planet equals the farthest of another is dismissed as weak. The author then raises the possibility of additional spheres beyond the ninth, known only to God, and gives an example: the epicycle of Mars is larger than the Sun, so it is conceivable to have even larger spheres. Finally, he critiques the ancient astronomers' method: they observed apparent order and concluded that the celestial spheres are unchanging, but this is not a demonstrative premise. Later scholars mistakenly thought it was a proof. The author implies that the correct approach is to recognize the limitations of empirical observation and not to treat such premises as certain.

السيارات
الكرة الثوابت

Classifying the Lowest Celestial Sphere
Classification of two possibilities for the lowest sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main possibilities for the lowest celestial sphere and their sub-options as classified in the passage.

Core Idea
The lowest sphere is either concentric or eccentric, with further subdivisions.

In this passage, the author lays out a classification (taqsīm) of the possible configurations for the lowest celestial sphere, likely the sphere of the moon. Understanding this logical division is essential for following later arguments about planetary motion in Islamic astronomy. Let's parse the branching structure step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of classification
The passage begins with 'بالأرض أول الكون' (the first [sphere] of the universe is adjacent to the earth). This is the lowest celestial sphere, i.e., the one closest to Earth.
The word 'falak' (sphere) is implied.
2
Extract the first division
The author gives two possibilities: either the sphere is centered at the center of the world (concentric) or outside it (eccentric). The Arabic: 'إما أن يكون مركزة مركز العالم وإما أن يكون خارجًا عن مركز العالم'.
This is a binary exclusive division.
3
Process the concentric branch
If concentric, there are two further possibilities: either the planet moves within the sphere ('محركًا فيه') or is composed/fixed in it ('مكونًا فيه'). The text is ambiguous due to OCR.
The second sub-option might mean the planet is fixed in the sphere.
4
Understand the eccentric branch
If eccentric, the passage states: 'فهو أن يكون الفلك خافق المركز والكوكب لا يكون مركزًا فيه بل في حرم مركز فيه يتحرك وهو فلك التدوير'. This means the sphere is eccentric and the planet is not centered in it but in a rotating sphere (epicycle).
This is the Ptolemaic model for lower planets.
5
Summarize the classification tree
Create a tree: Lowest sphere → either concentric or eccentric. If concentric → either planet moving in sphere or fixed. If eccentric → sphere eccentric with epicycle.
The author does not yet evaluate which is correct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالأرض أول الكون. فإن كان الأقل فأما أن يكون مركزة مركز العالم وإما أن يكون خارجًا عن مركز العالم. فإن كان الأول فإما أن يكون الكوكب محركًا فيه أو مكونًا [؟] المركون فيه. فإن كان الأول استمال أن يختلف بسبب ذلك وبعده عن الأرض. وإن كان الثاني فهو أن يكون الفلك خافق المركز والكوكب لا يكون مركزًا فيه بل في حرم مركز فيه يتحرك وهو فلك التدوير.
The first [sphere] of the universe is [adjacent to?] the earth. If it is the lowest, then either it is centered at the center of the world or it is outside the center of the world. If the first, then either the planet is moving in it or is composed [or configured?] in it. If the first, then it would differ due to that and its distance from the earth. And if the second, then the sphere is eccentric and the planet is not centered in it but in a rotating sphere which is the epicycle.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is a classification (taqsīm) of the possible configurations for the lowest celestial sphere, presumably the sphere of the moon. The author begins by stating that the first sphere is adjacent to the earth. He then divides: either this sphere is concentric with the earth (centered at the center of the world) or eccentric. If concentric, then either the planet moves within the sphere or is somehow fixed in it. If the first scenario (concentric and planet moving in it), then the planet's motion would vary due to its distance from the earth. If the second scenario (eccentric), then the sphere itself is eccentric and the planet is not centered in the sphere but rather in a separate rotating sphere called the epicycle (fak al-tadwīr). This classification is fundamental in Ptolemaic astronomy, which Islamic scholars like al-Razi discuss critically. The author does not yet evaluate these possibilities but sets them up for further argumentation.

Analyzing the Argument for the Imperceptibility of Earth's Curvature
Author argues why earth's curvature is not visible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the author's conditional argument for why the earth's curvature is not visible to the senses.

Core Idea
The small arc of a great circle appears straight, and vapors obscure distant bottoms.

In this lesson, we examine a passage from a work of Islamic natural philosophy where the author defends the sphericity of the earth against a sensory objection. He uses an analogy with a well and a geometric principle to explain why we do not see the curvature. This passage demonstrates how conditional reasoning is employed to reconcile theoretical knowledge with empirical observation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The author anticipates the objection: 'If the earth is curved, why don't we see its curvature?' This objection challenges the theoretical claim with sensory experience.
Recognize that this is a common skeptical move against non-obvious theoretical claims.
2
Examine the analogy with a well
The author compares seeing the bottom of a distant well to seeing the earth's curvature. He notes that the two distances (depth and surface) are different, and that something obstructs view of the bottom.
The analogy serves to introduce the concept of an obstacle (vapors) that prevents perception.
3
Extract the two reasons for imperceptibility
The author gives two reasons: (a) vapors rising from the earth near the surface form a thick layer that vision cannot penetrate, obscuring the bottom of the 'well'; (b) a small arc of a great circle appears straight to the senses, so the earth's curvature is below the threshold of perception.
These reasons function as the 'obstacles' in the conditional argument.
4
Reconstruct the conditional argument
Formalize: 'If there were no obstacles, the curvature would be visible.' But we observe that curvature is not visible. Therefore, there must be obstacles. This is modus tollens: deny the consequent to infer denial of the antecedent.
This reasoning shows that the failure to observe curvature does not disprove sphericity; it only confirms the presence of obstacles.
5
Evaluate the argument's strength
Consider whether the two reasons are sufficient. Are vapors always present? Does the small arc principle apply to the entire earth? This step involves critical assessment within the context of medieval natural philosophy.
Modern science confirms both points: atmospheric refraction and the scale of curvature make it imperceptible at ground level.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مباحث المال وهى سبعة فا ان شكله الله ذكر وانا حصن وجوها المئة ما تأبى البحران اقرب من خل ظرف له قلته أولائم اسفلة ثانيا مع ان البعدين وين القلة اكثر ما بينه وبين الاسفل ما خلال الالات جدية المار ما نعة ولقائل له نفر اسلم قلتم انه لا مانع إلا ما علم فإن ابجداب الذي نرى اسفلة والصلاة لم يكن رؤيتنا للت الموضوع لسفر ذلك الجدار كر وبين الله الموضوع في اعلاه فعرفنا بذلك ان هنا ما تعاونات كا ذكر بقوة ثم نقول لا لايجون ان يكون ذلك المانع هوان الابحة المتصاعدة من الارض القريبة من وجهها متكاتفة محددة للهواة فلا حرم لا ينفد البصر فيه فلايرى حضيض الحجل فاما قلته فان الاتحة الواصلة اليها قليلة فلا حمانا دايناها وانضافات القوس الصغيرة من الدائرة العظيمة تكون في الحس كا تخط المستقيم واذا كان كذلك فمن المستبعد ان يكون تحدب القوير التى
Investigations of the world, and they are seven. The first is its shape. He mentioned the aspects of the obstacle, that the two seas are closer than the bottom of a container, its depth and its surface second, although the two distances between the bottom and the lowest are more than between it and the surface. And a speaker can say: You said that there is no obstacle except what is known, for the wall whose bottom we see and the prayer, our vision of the entire object was not because of that wall being covered, and between the object placed at its top we knew by that that there is here something interlocking as mentioned strongly. Then we say: It is inevitable that that obstacle is the vapors rising from the earth near its surface, thick and limiting the air, so indeed vision does not penetrate it, so it does not see the bottom of the basin. As for its depth, the vapors reaching it are few, so they do not obstruct it. And the additions of the small arc of the great circle are in sensation like a straight line. And if that is so, it is improbable that the curvature of the earth is...
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage discusses the first investigation of the world: its shape. The author addresses a potential objection: if the earth were curved, why don't we see its curvature? He presents a response by analogy with a well or container. The author argues that our inability to see the bottom of a distant well is due to rising vapors obscuring the view, not because the earth is curved. He also notes that a small arc of a great circle appears straight to the senses, so we cannot perceive the earth's curvature directly. The reasoning is conditional: if there were no obstacle, we would see the curvature; but there are obstacles (vapors) and mathematical considerations (small arc appears straight).

Empirical Argument for Earth's Sphericity
Empirical argument for earth's sphericity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the empirical argument for the earth's sphericity and identify its premises and conclusion.

Core Idea
Water drops reveal the spherical nature of water—and by extension, the earth.

In this passage, the author presents a two-part empirical demonstration for the spherical shape of the earth. First, he argues that the curvature is gradual, not blocking mountain bases. Second, he uses the natural spherical shape of water drops on a smooth surface to infer that water tends toward sphericality. Together, these observations support the claim that the earth is spherical, as water covers most of its surface.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
What is the author trying to prove? The earth is spherical.
2
Find the curvature argument
How does the author argue that curvature does not block sight? He states it is improbable that the curvature prevents seeing the base of a mountain, implying the curve is gradual.
3
Find the water drop analogy
What observation is offered? Water drops on a smooth surface form spheres.
4
Link the analogy to the earth
How does water's spherical shape support earth's sphericity? Earth has large oceans; water tends to spherical shape, so earth's shape follows.
5
Evaluate the argument
Is this analogy sufficient? It assumes the earth is predominantly water, which is true, but land masses complicate the analogy. The argument is suggestive but not conclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن المستبعد أن يكون تحدب القوير التي بين الناظر وحضيض الجبل من جملة عود البحر المحيط بالأرض مانعاً من رؤية أسفل الجبل. وإذا صبنا قطرات الماء على سطح لطيف فإن تلك القطرات تتشكل بشكل الكرة.
It is improbable that the curvature of the earth between the observer and the base of the mountain, from the totality of the curve of the surrounding sea, prevents seeing the base of the mountain. And if we pour drops of water on a smooth surface, those drops take the shape of a sphere.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage presents an empirical argument for the sphericity of the earth. The author first states that it is improbable that the curvature of the earth prevents seeing the base of a mountain, implying that the curvature is gradual. Then, using an analogy, he notes that water drops naturally form spheres when placed on a smooth surface, indicating that water—and by extension the earth—tends toward a spherical shape. This is a typical physical demonstration in Aristotelian physics, used to support the claim that the element water is spherical.

Dialectical Structure in Natural Philosophy
Dialectical comparison of water and earth's coldness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the stages of a dialectical argument in a passage of Islamic natural philosophy and map them to the rules of jadal.

Core Idea
Debate sharpens truth: each claim faces objection, response, and counter.

This lesson unpacks a dialectical exchange from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas on whether water or earth is colder. The author presents an observation, then an objection, then a defense, followed by a counterargument from later scholars and a rebuttal. Understanding this structure trains the tool of jadal used in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
Find the proposition the author first asserts. In this passage, it is that water's coldness is greater than earth's, based on common observation.
The author says 'the observed fact is that its coldness is more than the coldness of earth'.
2
Locate the objection
The author anticipates a challenge: sensory strength does not always reflect reality. Point to the phrase 'Not everything that is stronger in sensation is stronger in reality'.
The example of melted metals feeling hotter than pure fire illustrates the objection.
3
Analyze the response
The author defends his claim by explaining why water cools more: it spreads and penetrates, while earth's density hinders contact.
This is a causal explanation based on physical properties.
4
Identify the later scholars' counterargument
Some later scholars argue that earth is fundamentally colder because density is an effect of cold and earth is farther from celestial motion (which brings heat).
This counter rests on a theoretical principle rather than direct observation.
5
Find the author's rebuttal
The author counters that density may have a different cause, and equal effects can arise from different causes. Thus earth's density does not prove greater coldness.
This shows the dialectical method: no argument is accepted without potential refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكن المشهود أن برده أكثر من برد الأرض، لأن الماء يستره فوق استره؟! ولقائل أن يقول: ليس كلما كان في الحس أقوى كان في الحقيقة أقوى، ألا ترى أن سخونة الفلزات الحاكة أقوى من سخونة النار الصرفة؟ والحشرات ليست أقوى منافٍ للحقيقة، لأن المكسور بالضد قد يكون أقوى من البطء. تفضيلات الماء للطاقة ينبسط على العضو فيصل إلى عمق كله بمنه ويلتصق به، أما التراب فلكثافته لا تصل إلى عمق العضو ولا تلتصق به بل يتناثر عنه سريعا، فلذلك كان تبريد الماء فوق تبريد الأرض. ومن المتأخرين من جعل برودة الأرض أقوى من برودة الماء لوجهنا: أن الأرض القمن المار والاكتف، أردلان الكثافة من آثار البرد، والأرض أبعد عن وصول الحركة الفلكية من الماء فكانت أبرد. ولقائل أن يقول: قوات على الأقل بأنه حات المزودة علة الكثافة، ولكن لا يلزم من ذلك أن الأعصر الكثافة ألكبارد، لجواز تعليل الحكمين المتساويين بعلتين مختلفتين، مجون أن يكون زيادة كثافة الأرض لزيادة برودتها بل لعلة أخرى، وعى الساق الله بنات أن سبب كثافة الأرض وبردها بعدها عن الحركة الفلكية، وقد أبطلناه في عود المراد. طبيعة الماء مقتضية للبرودة، والبر نقض للمحمود، فطبيعة الملمقتضية للجود، لكن الشمس إذا قربت من سمت الرأس سخنت الفلك الأرضي ونحن الهواء الملاصق لها منعاً طبيعة الماء من اقتضاء البرد، وإذا بعدت من سمت الرأس برد الماء وعلى العالي.
But the observed fact is that its coldness is more than the coldness of earth, because water covers it? And one may say: Not everything that is stronger in sensation is stronger in reality. Do you not see that the heat of melted metals is stronger than the heat of pure fire? And insects are not stronger opponents of truth because the broken by its opposite may be stronger than slowness. The preference of water for energy: it spreads over the organ, reaches its depth all of it by its nature, and adheres to it. As for earth, due to its density, it does not reach the depth of the organ nor adhere to it, but scatters quickly. Thus the cooling of water is above the cooling of earth. Among later scholars, some made the coldness of earth stronger than the coldness of water for our reason: that earth is ... density is from the effects of cold, and earth is farther from the reach of celestial motion than water, so it is colder. And one may say: ... it is the cause of density, but it does not follow that denser things are colder, because it is possible to explain two similar judgments by two different causes, such that the increase in earth's density is not for increase in its coldness but for another cause, and ... that the cause of earth's density and coldness is its distance from celestial motion, and we have refuted that elsewhere. The nature of water requires coldness, and coldness is contrary to heat, so the nature of ... requires heat, but when the sun approaches the zenith, it heats the terrestrial sphere and we heat the air adjacent to it, preventing water's nature from requiring cold, and when it moves away from the zenith, water cools and on the high.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage presents a critical argument about the relative cooling powers of water and earth. The author begins by noting the common observation that water feels colder than earth. He then anticipates an objection: sensory strength does not always reflect reality, with examples of metal feeling hotter than fire and insects paradoxically being stronger fighters. He then explains why water cools more: water spreads and penetrates deeply into an object, while earth is dense and does not adhere. Later scholars argued that earth is fundamentally colder because density is an effect of coldness, and earth is farther from celestial motion (which brings heat). The author presents a counterargument: density may have a different cause, and two equal effects can have different causes. He concludes by noting that water's nature is cold, but solar distance affects it. The discussion reflects a dialectical method typical of Islamic natural philosophy, weighing empirical observation against theoretical principles.

الكثافة
الحركة الفلكية

Checkpoint
What is the primary objection raised against the claim that water is colder than earth?
A) Water is too fluid to retain cold.
B) Sensory strength does not always reflect reality.
C) Earth is more dense than water.
D) The sun heats water more than earth.

Key Takeaway
Dialectical method uses structured stages – claim, objection, response, counter, rebuttal – to test propositions in Islamic natural philosophy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Map the dialectical structure of the argument in al-Razi's passage: identify the initial claim, objection, response, counterargument, and rebuttal, and label each according to the rules of adab al-bahth.
?

Causal Reasoning: Sensation vs Reality
Critique of sensory and causal reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical fallacy of equating sensory intensity with real intensity and analyse causal claims about density and coldness.

Core Idea
Stronger sensation does not guarantee stronger reality: causes can mislead.

Al-Razi's passage warns that what we sense may not correspond to the true nature of things. He uses the example of metals feeling hotter than fire, and then critiques the argument that density proves coldness. This lesson trains you to spot such causal fallacies and to assess whether one property genuinely follows another.

Guided Path
1
Extract the principle
Identify the key epistemological claim: 'Not everything that is stronger in sensation is stronger in reality.' This is a caution against trusting raw sensory data.
The author provides an example: melted metals feel hotter than pure fire, yet they are not truly hotter.
2
Examine the analogy
Consider how the example of metals challenges the initial claim about water and earth. If sensory data can mislead about heat, can it also mislead about cold?
The author uses this to weaken the reliance on common observation for the coldness comparison.
3
Analyze the density-coldness argument
Later scholars argued: density is an effect of coldness, earth is denser, so earth is colder. Identify the logical steps.
The argument assumes that if density is a sign of cold, then denser means colder.
4
Assess the refutation
The author objects that two equal effects (here, density and coldness) may arise from different causes. Thus, density does not necessarily imply coldness.
This is a logical point: correlation does not prove causation, and one effect can have multiple causes.
5
Reflect on causation in Islamic sciences
Consider how this reasoning applies to usul al-fiqh: when jurists use 'illa (cause) to extend rulings, they must ensure the cause is exclusive.
Al-Razi's point aligns with conditions for valid qiyas (analogical reasoning).

Source Text

Sharing Accidents Does Not Imply Same Essence
Author refutes claim that celestial fire is dry using essential vs. accidental distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that shared accidental properties do not entail shared essence in evaluating arguments about the nature of things.

Core Idea
Accidents can be common while essences remain distinct.

In this passage, the author refutes an opponent's claim that celestial fire is dry because it shares heat and subtlety with air. The refutation hinges on a crucial logical distinction: sharing accidental properties does not prove that two things have the same essence. This principle is fundamental in ontology and logic, and it protects us from conflating things that merely appear similar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim and evidence
The opponent claims celestial fire is dry. Their evidence is that fire and air share accidents like heat and subtlety, implying they share the same essence. Find this in the passage where it says 'sharing in heat and subtlety'.
The literal text: 'اشتراك الهواء والنار في الحرارة والرقة'
2
Extract the logical principle
The author states the general rule: sharing in accidents does not necessitate sharing in essence. This is the qānūn (rule) that governs the refutation. Identify the Arabic phrase: 'الاشتراك في العوارض لا يوجب الاشتراك في الماهية'.
3
Understand the distinction between essence and accident
Essence (māhiyya) is what makes a thing what it is; accidents ('awāriḍ) are contingent properties that can change without altering the thing's identity. For example, water remains water whether hot or cold. Accidents can be shared by different essences.
4
Apply the principle to the case at hand
Although air and fire share the accidents of heat and subtlety, their essences are different. Air is not fire; celestial fire is a different kind of body. Therefore, the conclusion that celestial fire is dry does not follow from these shared accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقائلة بأنها يابسة باطلة لاشكل كونها حادة فهي إما أن تكون طبيعة اليابسة فإن كانت طبيعة كانت هواء فكان يجب أن لا تتحرك بالطبع عن حيز الهواء وإن كانت يابسة وهو المطلوب والقانون أن الاشتراك في العوارض لا يوجب الاشتراك في الماهية فلا يلزم من اشتراك الهواء والنار في الحرارة والرقة اشتراكهما في تمام الماهية
And the claim that it is dry is false, because it has no [shape?] that it is sharp. So it is either the nature of the dry; if it were the nature [of the dry] it would be air, then it would necessarily not move naturally from the place of air; and if it were dry, that is what is required. And the rule is that sharing in accidents does not necessitate sharing in essence, so it does not follow from the sharing of air and fire in heat and subtlety that they share in the complete essence.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of an opponent's claim that celestial fire is dry (yābisa). The author presents an objection: if celestial fire were dry, it would have to be either of the nature of the dry element (which is earth, or perhaps air? The text says 'طبيعة اليابسة' but then says it would be air, so possibly a confusion in the text). Actually, the argument seems to be: if it were dry, it would be solid and not naturally move upward, but it does. The opponent might argue that celestial fire shares accidents like heat and subtlety with air, so it must share essence. The author refutes this by stating the logical principle (qānūn) that sharing accidental properties does not imply sharing in essence. This is a crucial point in ontology and logic: essence (māhiyya) is what makes a thing what it is, while accidents ('awāriḍ) are contingently affiliated. For example, water and wine both share the property of being liquid, but they have different essences. In the Islamic sciences, this principle is used in kalām to distinguish God's attributes from His essence, and in fiqh to differentiate between essential and accidental rulings. Here, it clarifies that celestial fire's heat is accidental, not essential, thus it can be unlike sublunary fire.

Key Terms
العوارض
الماهية
الاشتراك

Reconstructing Razi's Disjunctive Proof for a Breaking Nature
Razi presents three disjunctive arguments proving a common nature that breaks elemental qualities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Razi's three disjunctive arguments and explain why a separate breaking nature is necessary for elemental mixture.

Core Idea
Mixture requires a nature that breaks elemental qualities — Razi proves it by disjunctive syllogisms.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, in his discussion of mixture (imtizaj), confronts a key problem: how do the four elements with their opposed qualities combine? He offers three disjunctive arguments to prove that there must be a distinct nature, not itself an element, that breaks their qualities. This lesson will walk you through the logical structure of those arguments, using the disjunctive syllogisms familiar from your study of qiyas istithna'i munfasil.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem of mixture
Razi asks: when elements mix, what happens to their primary qualities (hot, cold, wet, dry)? They must be 'broken' (تكسر) to allow a new composite. But how?
2
Analyze the first disjunction: two forms or one?
Razi considers whether heat and dryness in the fiery nature are two separate forms or one. If two, matter would have more than one form (impossible). If one, then the other quality needs a different subject. Thus a separate matter for the four qualities is needed.
This disjunction is of the form 'either both are forms together, or only one is a form.'
3
Analyze the second disjunction on the mode of breaking
When qualities break during mixture, either they break successively or simultaneously. Successive breaking is impossible because a broken quality cannot break another (الاستحالة أن يعود المكسور بعد انكساره كاسرا). Simultaneous breaking would require each to be caused by the other's repulsion, but cause and effect must coexist—yet the repulsion of one is not present when the other is breaking.
Both horns of the disjunction lead to contradiction.
4
Conclude the necessity of a third nature
Since both possible modes of breaking are impossible, there must be a separate nature that breaks all qualities. This is Razi's 'breaking nature' (طبيعة كاسرة).
The conclusion is that a fifth nature, distinct from the four elements, is required.
5
Relate to the broader context
Razi notes that these investigations were covered in the chapter on motion, so he is building on prior conclusions. The argument also presupposes the reality of generation and corruption (الكون والفساد).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لثلثة أوجه في هذه الأعراض قابلة للاشتداد والنقص والطبيعة النارية غير قابلة للهلاك. ليست المادة بأن تكون حرارة دون يبوسة أولى من العكس. فإما أن تكونا صورتين معا فتقوم المادة بأكثر من الصورة الواحدة، هذا خلف. أو تكون واحدة منهما صورة، فلابد من شيء آخر ليكون مادة لهذه الأربعة. إذا اختلطت انكسرت كيفية كل واحد منها إلا على سبيل الانكسارات. إما أن تكون بعضها سابقا على البعض وهو محال، والاستحالة أن يعود المكسور بعد انكساره كاسرا، أو معا، فيكون انكسار كل واحد منها معللا بصرف كيفية الآخر، لأن العلة حاصلة مع المعلول، وصرف شيء منها غير حاصل عند انكسار شيء منها. فلا بد من طبيعة أخرى هي الكاسرة لهذه الكيفيات، وهو المطلوب. وعلى هذه الوجوه الثلاثة كلمات يمكنك تعرف الحال فيها من نفسك بعد وقوفك على ما سلف في صحة الكون والفساد والاستحالة على أجزاء كل واحد من هذه الأربعة. قد تم هذان البحثان في باب الحركة فلا تعيدهما. اللهم فتح الكون والفساد والاستحالة عليها بأسها. إما الفتح على جميعها محال لأنه لو فرق صور كلبات هذه العناصر لكان قد حدثت لها صور في سوى هذه الأربعة، فيكون الأركان سوى هذه الأربعة، وهو مستبعد جدا.
There are three aspects concerning these qualities that are susceptible to intensification and diminution, while the fiery nature is not susceptible to destruction. It is not that matter should have heat without dryness more likely than the opposite. For either the two are two forms together, then matter would subsist by more than one form—this is a contradiction; or one of them is a form, then there must be another thing to be matter for these four. When they mix, the quality of each one is broken except by way of breakings. Either some precede others, which is impossible—and it is impossible for the broken to become a breaker after being broken—or they occur together, in which case the breaking of each is caused by the repulsion of the quality of the other, because the cause coexists with the effect, and the repulsion of something is not present when the breaking of something else occurs. So there must be another nature that breaks these qualities, and that is what is sought. Concerning these three aspects, there are words by which you can know the situation from yourself after your knowledge of what preceded regarding the truth of generation, corruption, and substantial change concerning the parts of each of these four. These two investigations have already been completed in the chapter on motion, so do not repeat them. O God, open generation, corruption, and substantial change upon them fully. As for opening over all of them, it is impossible because if the forms of the natures of these elements were dispersed, forms would have occurred to them other than these four, and then the pillars would be other than these four, which is very unlikely.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument about the nature of elemental qualities when they mix. The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, is addressing a problem in natural philosophy: how do the four elements (earth, water, air, fire) with their primary qualities (hot, cold, wet, dry) mix to form composite bodies? He argues that there must be a distinct nature that 'breaks' the qualities of the elements to allow mixture. The argument proceeds through three disjunctive steps (ثلثة أوجه). First, the qualities (like heat and dryness) are subject to increase and decrease, but the fiery nature itself is not destroyed. Second, it is not that matter is more appropriately hot without dry than the opposite; this symmetry leads to a disjunction: either heat and dryness are two separate forms, which is impossible because matter cannot have two forms simultaneously, or one is a form and the other is not, requiring an additional subject (matter) for the four qualities. Third, when elements mix, their qualities are broken. This breaking cannot be successive (some before others) because then a broken quality would have to break another, which is absurd; nor can it be simultaneous because then each quality's breaking would be caused by the repulsion of the other, but cause and effect would not coexist properly. Therefore, there must be a third nature that breaks the qualities. The author concludes that this is what is sought (وهو المطلوب). He then notes that these investigations were already covered in the chapter on motion. The argument uses classical logical forms—disjunctive syllogism and reductio ad absurdum—to establish a necessary metaphysical principle. In the Islamic intellectual tradition, this type of reasoning is crucial for understanding how the physical world operates, and it mirrors the logical rigor applied in fields like kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
طبيعة كاسرة (tabī‘a kāsira): A nature or substance that breaks or overcomes the qualities of elements during mixture, allowing them to combine into a new composite. [Note: This term is specific to Razi's physical theory; later philosophers like al-Tusi refer to 'mizaj' (temperament) rather than a separate breaking nature.]
كيفيات (kayfiyyāt): Primary qualities (hot, cold, wet, dry) that characterize the four elements and are subject to intensification and diminution. [Note: In logic, 'kayfiyya' often means 'quality' as a category; here it is physical quality.]
الكون والفساد (al-kawn wa al-fasād): Generation (coming into being) and corruption (passing away), the processes by which substances change in the sublunary world. [Note: Standard Aristotelian terms used in Islamic philosophy; stable across texts.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi's argument in this passage, why cannot the breaking of elemental qualities occur successively?
A) Because a cause must coexist with its effect.
B) Because a broken quality cannot break another.
C) Because matter cannot have more than one form.
D) Because heat and dryness are always together.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves the existence of a breaking nature by eliminating all other logical possibilities through disjunctive arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a disjunctive syllogism based on the first disjunction in Razi's argument (the two possibilities for heat and dryness). Use the form: either A or B; not A; therefore B. What are A and B? Then explain why the conclusion leads to the need for a second nature.
?
In the jadal tradition, the objection that the breaking of qualities could be successive is rejected by Razi as impossible (المحال). Write a brief jadal exchange where a student objects that the breakings could be successive, and the teacher refutes it using Razi's reasoning.

The Definition of Mizaj as an Intermediate Quality
Definition of mixture as an intermediate quality from element interaction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in Razi's definition of mizaj and evaluate whether the definition is complete.

Core Idea
Mizaj is an intermediate quality, not an extreme.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi defines mixture (mizaj) as a quality that results from the mutual interaction of the four elements. By analyzing his definition, you will see how a logical definition uses genus and differentia. This tool is essential for precise conceptual analysis in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined (definiendum)
The term being defined is 'al-mizaj' (mixture). Razi states: 'The reality of mixture is...'
2
Extract the genus
What broader category does mizaj belong to? It is a 'quality' (kayfiyya), as Razi says 'a quality similar in all, intermediate between [the qualities].'
3
Extract the differentia
What distinguishes mizaj from other qualities? It is intermediate between extremes and arises from the mutual overcoming of opposing elements.
4
Check completeness
Does this definition include all mixtures and exclude non-mixtures? Yes, because every genuine mixture yields a uniform intermediate quality, while mere aggregates do not.
5
Compare with alternative definitions
Later in the passage, Razi discusses whether elements retain their natures; this does not change the definition of mizaj itself but affects how we understand the resultant quality.

Source Text

The following chapter on actions and passions. Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on actions and passions. The author defines mixture (mizaj) as an intermediate quality that results from the mutual interaction of the four elements when they come together. Each element acts on the other, and a uniform quality is produced that is intermediate between the extremes of the elements. The author then discusses that this interaction requires contact, but gives examples like the sun's heat and sensory perception to show that interaction can occur without direct contact. He then addresses a debate: Do elements retain their original natures when forming a compound, or do they perish and a new nature arise? The Peripatetics held that the natures remain but are modified, while later scholars argued that a single new nature replaces them. The author seems to side with a middle view, emphasizing that the natures are not the same as the qualities.

The definition of mizaj as an intermediate quality between the extremes of the elements is a valid logical definition that satisfies the conditions of genus and differentia.

The Debate on Elemental Natures in Mixtures
Debate on whether elements retain natures in a mixture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Peripatetic and later views on whether elements retain their natures in a mixture and explain the basis of the debate.

Core Idea
Do elemental natures persist or perish? The answer hinges on whether natures are distinct from qualities.

After defining mixture, Razi delves into a philosophical controversy: do the four elements keep their original natures when combined, or do these natures vanish and a single new nature emerge? This debate has profound implications for natural philosophy and medicine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing positions
Razi names two camps: the Peripatetics, who hold that the natures of elements remain in the compound, and the later scholars (mutākhkharūn), who maintain that the natures perish and a single new nature arises.
2
Find the basis of the debate
Razi says this discussion branches from the fact that the natures (ṭabāʾiʿ) of elements are not identical to their qualities (kayfiyyāt). If they were identical, the debate would not arise.
3
Analyze the Peripatetic view
The Peripatetics believe that when elements mix, only the qualities are affected (they become intermediate), but the underlying natures remain intact. Thus, a mixture retains elements with their distinct natures.
4
Analyze the later scholars' view
These scholars argue that the natures themselves are destroyed and replaced by a single, new nature for the compound. This explains why a compound behaves as a unified substance.
5
Evaluate Razi's position
Razi seems to favor a middle path or the later view, as he mentions 'a conclusive proof' that the natures are not the qualities themselves. However, the text is too corrupted to determine his final verdict fully.

Source Text

Defining Maturation and Putrefaction with Their Four Causes
Definition of maturation and putrefaction with their four causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of maturation and putrefaction and list their four causes (material, efficient, formal, final).

Core Idea
Change is defined by its end: preservation or corruption; the four causes explain why.

In this passage from his Mulakhkhas, al-Razi defines two fundamental physical processes: maturation (al-nuḍj) and putrefaction (al-taʿfīn). He provides precise definitions using genus and differentia, then analyzes each through the four Aristotelian causes. Understanding this causal framework is essential for Islamic medicine and natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Find the Arabic definition of maturation (النضج) and putrefaction (التعفين). Note the genus (change/إحالة) for each and the differentia (conditions that distinguish them).
Maturation: change by heat in moist body toward intended purpose. Putrefaction: change of moisture to a strange quality not intended.
2
Compare the opposites
The author lists two things opposite to maturation: one is privation (like fruit remaining raw), the other is putrefaction. Understand the difference between absence of change and positive corruption.
Privation is not a change; putrefaction is a change to a corrupt quality.
3
Extract the material cause
For both maturation and putrefaction, the material cause (العلة المادية) is the moist body. Why does the author specify the same matter for both?
Same matter can lead to different outcomes depending on other causes.
4
Extract the efficient cause
For maturation, the efficient cause (العلة الفاعلية) is heat; for putrefaction, it is absence of heat or cold. Note the asymmetry: one is positive, the other privative.
Heat directs moisture toward intention; its absence leads to corruption.
5
Extract the formal and final causes
For maturation, the formal cause is conditioning of moisture by a natural quality; for putrefaction, it is privation of that conditioning. The final cause for maturation is preservation of individuals; for putrefaction, it is corruption.
The final cause gives the 'why' – does the process serve life or lead to decay?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه الأمور فى النضج حده أن إحالة من الحرارة للجسم ذي الرطوبة إلى موافقة الغاية المقصودة، وهو طبيعي وصناعي. فالطبيعي قسمين: نضح الضروريات للغذاء، وهو إما نضج ما يحتاج إلى جنسه كالثمار، أو نضج الفضول والمحتاج إلى دفعه، وهو نضح الفضل. ونضج الثمرة وهو أن يصير بحيث يولد المثل إن كان من شأنه ذلك. والمقابل للنضج أمران: أحدهما كالعدم، وهو أن تبقى الرطوبة غير مسلوك بها إلى الغاية المقصودة مع أنها لا تكون قد استحالت إلى كيفية منافية للغاية المقصودة مثل بقاء الثمرة نيئة وبقاء الغذاء غير متخمر عن حاله. وثانيها أن تصل تلك الرطوبة إلى كيفية غريبة، وهي إن قويت مع التحليل الرطوبات كان ذلك [؟]، وإن لم يقو ذلك بل على إحالتها لا إلى ما يوافق الغاية المقصودة كان تعفينا. ومنتهى العفونة إما اليبس أو ضرورة الحرارة التي كانت عفونية بالنسبة إلى الأول غريزية بالنسبة إلى ما هي فيه. واعلم أن سبب التين الثاني والثالث حرارة غريبة عن الشيء. فأسباب النضج: أما المادية فالجسم الرطب، والفاعلية فالحرارة، والصورية فتكيف الرطوبة بكيفية تعرض للطبيعة، والغائية قسمة نسو الأشخاص. وأسباب التعفين: مادته الجسم الرطب، وفاعله عدم الحادة أو البرودة، وصورته بقاء الرطوبة غير مسلوك بها إلى الغاية الطبيعية وهو أمر عدمي، وغايته الفساد.
These matters concerning maturation: its definition is that it is a change by heat in a body having moisture to conformity with the intended purpose, and it is natural and artificial. The natural is of two kinds: maturation of necessities for nourishment, which is either maturation of what needs its kind like fruits, or maturation of superfluities and what needs to be expelled, which is maturation of surplus. And maturation of the fruit is that it becomes such that it generates its like if it is of its nature. Opposite to maturation are two things: one is like non-existence, which is that the moisture remains not directed toward the intended purpose while it has not changed into a quality contrary to the intended purpose, like the fruit remaining raw and the nourishment remaining unfermented in its state. The second is that the moisture arrives at a strange quality, and if it strengthens with the analysis of moistures, that is [؟]; if it does not strengthen but rather changes it not to what agrees with the intended purpose, it is putrefaction. The end of putrefaction is either dryness or necessity of heat that was putrefactive relative to the first but innate relative to what it is in. Know that the cause of the second and third is heat foreign to the thing. The causes of maturation: material cause is the moist body, efficient cause is heat, formal cause is the conditioning of moisture by a quality that occurs to nature, final cause is the preservation of individuals. The causes of putrefaction: its matter is the moist body, its efficient cause is absence of heat or cold, its form is the remaining of moisture not directed toward the natural purpose, which is a privative thing, and its end is corruption.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage defines maturation (النضج) and its opposite putrefaction (التعفين), then analyzes each through the four causes (material, efficient, formal, final). The author begins by defining maturation as the change by heat in a moist body toward a desired end. He divides it into natural and artificial. Natural maturation has two types: one for the necessities of nourishment (like fruits ripening or food digesting) and another for expelling superfluities (like purging). He then gives two opposites: the first is a lack of maturation (like a fruit remaining raw), which is a privation; the second is a change to a quality not intended, which leads to putrefaction. Putrefaction results when heat is either excessive in a putrefactive way or is foreign to the body. The author then lists the four causes for maturation: material (moist body), efficient (heat), formal (moisture conditioned for nature), final (preservation of species). For putrefaction: material (moist body), efficient (absence of heat or cold), formal (moisture not directed naturally, a privation), final (corruption). This causal analysis follows Aristotelian physics and is used here to distinguish natural from corruptive change.

Key Terms
النضج
التعفين
العلة المادية
العلة الفاعلية
العلة الصورية
العلة الغائية

Checkpoint

A) Heat
B) Cold
C) Absence of heat or cold
D) Moisture

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi defines maturation as change toward intention and putrefaction as change away from it, and explains both through four causes.

Snow and Hail: Timing of Freezing
Al-Razi distinguishes snow from hail by timing of freezing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between snow and hail based on al-Razi's criterion of whether freezing occurs before or after droplet formation.

Core Idea
Snow freezes before droplets form; hail freezes after.

This passage from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's Mulakhkhas classifies precipitation by two main factors: the severity of cold and the stage at which it acts on vapor. We focus on the first distinction: when cold is severe, the outcome depends on timing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two outcomes of severe cold
Al-Razi says that if cold reaches vapor before its parts gather into droplets, snow falls; if after, hail falls. Mark these two conditions.
The key is the order relative to droplet formation.
2
Clarify the meaning of 'gathering into grains'
The phrase 'اجزاء البخار قبل اجتماعها والخلافها حبات' means the vapor particles have not yet coagulated into water droplets. This is the state before condensation.
3
Contrast with the alternative
If cold acts after the droplets have formed, they freeze as hail. This distinction is purely temporal.
4
Visualize the mechanism
Draw a timeline: vapor rises -> either cold freezes it immediately (snow) or it condenses into droplets then freezes (hail).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if the cold is severe, then either the cold reaches the parts of the vapor before their gathering and forming into grains, or after their forming likewise. If the first, snow falls; if the second, hail falls. And if the vapors do not reach the cold layer, then they are either abundant or little. If they are abundant, they either form a great cloud for a long time, having formed either due to winds [?] so that the wind compresses them together because of mountains blocking the wind, or there are opposing winds preventing the ascent of the vapors [?] or [?] then they stick together [?] due to intense cold of the air [?]. And what does not form a cloud at all is fog. And if the little vapors [?] are few and subtle, then [?] after the night [?] [?] [?] if they do not freeze, they become dew; if they freeze, they become frost.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies various forms of atmospheric moisture (precipitation) based on the conditions of vapor and cold. The author (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) presents a logical distinction: when cold is severe, it reaches the vapor either before the vapor particles combine into droplets (resulting in snow) or after they combine (resulting in hail). If the vapors do not ascend to the cold layer, there are two possibilities: either the vapors are abundant, forming clouds (or if they do not condense into a cloud, they become fog), or the vapors are little and subtle, leading to dew if not frozen, and frost if frozen. This is a classification based on causal conditions: the stage of vapor aggregation relative to cold, and the quantity and temperature of the vapor. The author uses a tree of distinctions to cover all common forms of precipitation seen in nature. This passage is typical of natural philosophy (physics) within the Aristotelian tradition, aiming to give a rational account of observable phenomena.

Key Terms

بخار (bukhār) vapor
تلج (talj) snow
برد (barad) hail

C) The wind speed
D) The time of day

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's classification of snow vs. hail hinges on a single temporal criterion: whether freezing precedes or follows droplet condensation.

Clouds and Fog: Abundant Vapors
Al-Razi classifies clouds and fog from abundant vapors below the cold layer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between cloud and fog based on whether abundant vapors condense into a distinct cloud or remain diffuse.

Core Idea
Abundant vapors that condense form clouds; if they do not condense, they form fog.

Al-Razi continues his classification by considering vapors that do not reach the cold layer. If they are abundant, they may either form a cloud (driven by winds or blocked by mountains) or, if they do not condense, become fog. Let's parse this part.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for this branch
The text says: if vapors do not reach the cold layer, then consider their quantity. Here we look at the case of abundant vapors.
2
Recognize the two sub-conditions for clouds
Clouds form either because winds compress the vapor (due to mountains blocking wind) or because opposing winds prevent ascent, causing vapor to accumulate and condense.
The OCR is unclear, but the basic idea is mechanical compression.
3
Identify what constitutes fog
Fog is defined as vapor that is abundant but does not condense into a distinct cloud. It remains as a diffuse mist.
4
Contrast with the earlier branch
Note that here cold is absent (vapors do not reach cold layer), so no freezing occurs. The vapor merely condenses into liquid droplets (cloud) or remains as vapor (fog).

Dew and Frost: Little Vapors
Al-Razi classifies dew and frost from little vapors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between dew and frost based on whether the little vapors freeze or remain liquid.

Core Idea
Little vapors that do not freeze become dew; if they freeze, they become frost.

The final part of al-Razi's classification deals with little and subtle vapors after night. He considers whether they freeze or not. Let's analyze this concise but important distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for this branch
The text says: if the vapors are little and subtle, and after night, they either freeze or not. This is the third main category after severe cold and abundant vapors.
2
Distinguish dew from frost
If they do not freeze, they become dew (طل). If they freeze, they become frost (صقيع). The sole criterion is the presence or absence of freezing.
The OCR is weak, but the contrast is clear.
3
Relate to the earlier distinction
Unlike snow and hail, here the cold is not severe (vapors are little and do not reach the cold layer). The freezing is presumably mild, occurring at ground level.
4
Consider the importance of the 'little' quantity
Al-Razi specifies that the vapors are little and subtle. This contrasts with abundant vapors that form clouds. Quantity determines the scale of the phenomenon.

Analyzing Causal Definitions in Natural Philosophy
Author explains causes of rainbow, thunder, lightning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definiendum and the cause (sabab) in a definition of a natural phenomenon and assess whether the definition is complete.

Core Idea
A complete definition of a natural phenomenon must state both what it is and why it occurs.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi explains the physical causes behind the rainbow, thunder, and lightning. These explanations are not mere descriptions; they are causal definitions (hadd) that give the essence and the reason. As students of logic, we learn how to dissect such definitions: what is being defined, what cause is proposed, and whether the explanation is logically sound. This lesson will train you to extract the logical structure behind the author's causal claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definienda
List the phenomena being defined: rainbow (qaws quzah), thunder (ra'd), lightning (barq). Note the halo (hālah) is mentioned but less defined.
The passage begins with al-Jawhari's definition of the rainbow.
2
Extract the cause (sabab) for each
For each phenomenon, find the stated cause: rainbow due to reflection from particles arranged around the sun; thunder due to compression of air from cloud explosion; lightning due to friction of dry and moist parts igniting vapor.
The text uses 'yuhdath' (occurs from) to indicate the cause.
3
Determine the type of cause
In Aristotelian terms, the cause given is the efficient cause (al-'illa al-fā'ila): the agent that produces the phenomenon.
The author does not discuss material, formal, or final causes here.
4
Check if the definition is complete
A complete definition includes all essential characteristics. For the rainbow, does reflection alone account for colors? The text mentions reflection, but later science adds refraction. Similarly, for lightning, friction is cited but electrical discharge is the modern cause.
Logical completeness requires that the cause is both necessary and sufficient.
5
Assess logical coherence
Are the causal chains consistent? For instance, lightning is from friction generating heat; thunder is from explosion. Are these consistent with each other? Does the explanation avoid circularity?
The passage does not explicitly connect thunder and lightning, though they often occur together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الجوهري قوس قزح التي في السمان علي العراق. والسبب في استدارة هذه القوس إن الأقرا التي تعكر عنها شعاع البصر وقعت بحيث لو انا جعلنا الشمس كز دائرة كان العدد الذي تقع من تلك الناثرة فوق الارض ممن على ملك الاجزار. فان كانت الشمس علا الافق كان الخط المات بالناظر عالية على بسيط الافق وهو المحود فيكون حينين في الافق يقسم المنطقة بنصفين قرى القوس نصف دائرة. وسبب استقامها العالما ان كون قطعا صغار امت وان كاد فرويت منسقية وإقالات حقال الناظر حيث يرى المتحدب مستقيما. وأما الرعد والبرق: فإن الرعد يحدث من تخلخل الهواء بسبب انفجار السحاب، والبرق يحدث من احتكاك الأجزاء اليابسة والرطبة في السحاب مع الحركة الشديدة، فيشتعل الدخان فيكون برقا.
Al-Jawhari said: 'The rainbow in the sky is over Iraq.' The reason for the curvature of this bow is that the particles which reflect the visual ray are positioned such that if we made the sun the center of a circle, the number of those reflecting particles above the earth would be on the circle of the ends. So if the sun is above the horizon, the line from the observer is high above the plane of the horizon, and it is the boundary; thus there are two halves in the horizon dividing the zone into two halves, so the bow appears as a semicircle. The reason for its straightness is that the small broken pieces, when seen from a distance, appear straight due to the observer's perspective. As for thunder and lightning: thunder occurs from the compression of air due to the explosion of the cloud, and lightning occurs from the friction of dry and moist parts in the cloud with violent motion, so the vapor ignites and becomes lightning.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the physical causes of three atmospheric phenomena: the rainbow, the halo (implied), thunder, and lightning. It begins by citing al-Jawhari's definition of the rainbow. Then it provides a geometric explanation for the semicircular shape of the rainbow: the reflecting particles are arranged around the sun as a center, and the observer's line of sight creates a semicircle. It also explains why the rainbow appears straight under certain conditions: small particles at a distance appear straight due to perspective. Then it turns to thunder and lightning: thunder is caused by the violent expansion of air as a cloud bursts, while lightning results from the friction of dry and moist components in the cloud, igniting vapor. The explanation follows a causal chain: friction generates heat, which ignites the vapor, producing lightning; the sound (thunder) accompanies the explosion. This discussion is typical of natural philosophy, applying logical reasoning to physical phenomena.

Key Terms
قوس قزح
هالة
رعد
برق

Checkpoint

B) Friction of dry and moist parts in a cloud

D) The compression of air

Key Takeaway
Causal definitions in natural philosophy follow a pattern: definiendum + efficient cause; evaluating their completeness requires checking if the cause suffices and is non-circular.

Classifying Springs: Conditions for Flowing, Stagnant, and Canal Water
Classification of springs and explanation of earthquakes and mountain formation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions that determine the type of a spring and explain how the most common cause of earthquakes relates to spring formation.

Core Idea
The type of spring depends on the presence or absence of three specific conditions; trapped vapor is the engine behind both earthquakes and springs.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi examines the physical causes behind the formation of springs and mountains. He distinguishes between flowing springs, stagnant springs, and canal water based on a set of conditions. He also introduces the concept of 'al-sabab al-akthari' (the most common cause) for earthquakes and spring formation: the accumulation of vapor underground. This lesson will help you apply the method of defining natural phenomena by their causal conditions, a key skill in Islamic natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions for a flowing spring
From the Arabic, the author implies three conditions (quyud) that must be present for a flowing spring to emerge. The first condition is that the earth is abundant and strong (كثيرة قوية). The second and third conditions are implied by their absence; try to infer them from the text.
Due to OCR corruption, the exact conditions are unclear, but the logic of missing conditions is evident.
2
Map the missing conditions to spring types
If only the third condition is missing, the spring is stagnant (العيون الراكدة). If both the second and third are missing, it becomes canal water (رمياه القناة). This shows a hierarchical dependence.
The text says 'فإن فات القيد الثالث فقط' for stagnant, and 'فإن فات الثاني أيضا أي الاستماع' for canal water.
3
Analyze the debate on the origin of water
The author mentions a dispute: whether spring water originates from scattered mineral particles or from transforming air. He leans toward the air theory, noting that the earth's density traps vapors.
This debate illustrates how natural philosophers used reasoning to choose between competing explanations.
4
Understand the most common cause (السبب الأكثرى)
The author identifies trapped vapor as the most common cause of earthquakes and springs. When vapor cannot escape, it builds up, moves the earth, and sometimes splits it, causing earthquakes and sounds.
This is an example of identifying a primary cause in natural phenomena.
5
Apply the causal definition to mountain formation
Mountains are formed when heat consolidates clay, similar to pumice. This shows that the same causal principles apply to different geological features.
The passage connects spring formation to mountain formation through the role of underground processes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طابع المكان الأمة إلى الأرض إن كانت كثيرة قوية على تفجير الأرض حيث ستتبع كرم من هاجرء فحدثت العيون السيالة. فإن فات القيد الثالث فقط حدثت العيون الراكدة. فإن فات الثاني أيضا أي الاستماع فهي رمياه القناة لأنها متولدة من أخرة ناقص من تو المريض فإذا النيل عروجها نقل التراب ما وقت تلك الأخرة منفذا فاندفعت الله بأدنى حكة. فإن أضيف إليه ماعده واسلة لقوات الإنسان خلاف في أن هذه المياه متولدة عن الأجزاء المانية المنفرقة في عمق الأرض أو هواء يتقلب كان والأقل أولى بالأكثرقة في الر ليلة لذا توا تحت الأرض خارج خاني حآت كثير المادة وكان وجه الأرض متكاتفاً عديم المسام فإذا حاول ذلك البخار الخروج ولم تمكن منه لكثافة وجه الأرض فجند قول ن ذاته ويحرك الأرض وربما كان القوة إلى أن يشق الأرض وربما الفصل نات المحرقة وحدثت أصواتا هائلة وهذا هو السبب الأكثرى. وههنا الباب أف قليلة مثل أن يكون في باطن الأرض أعواد فيسيل إليها مياة عظيمة أو تخدم قطعة عظيمة منها فت قلقل الهواء الذي تحت الأرض وقد يكون السقوط قلد الجبال عندكررة الاقتصاد الرطبها وسهولة انفصال بعضها عن بعض وعند قلتها الجفافها وسهولة تفتها وبالله العظيمة والتوفيق. النات النادي ع المال في المعادن وهو ما بينا في أي سبب التجو الطير الدرج إذا عملت الحرارة فيه حتى استحكمت الممازحة بين طبعه ويابسه ما رحى الكون الفقاع في تكون الجبال الحد العظيم أن يصادف طينا كثير الريجا أما دفعة أو على مرور الأيام عقده مجرا عظيما والسبب الاعترى ارتفاع الجبارات الطين بعد تحية مختلف أجزاؤه.
The imprint of the place of the community to the earth, if it is abundant and strong, on the explosion of the earth where the generosity of the emigrant follows, then flowing springs occur. If only the third condition is missing, then stagnant springs occur. If the second also is missing, i.e., the listening, then it is the water of the canal, because it is generated from a deficient final part of the patient's moisture, so when the Nile rises, the transport of soil at that time made an outlet for that final part, so the water gushed forth with the slightest itch. If what is added to it is a container and a utensil for human forces, there is a dispute whether these waters are generated from the scattered mineral parts in the depth of the earth or from air that transforms; and the lesser is more appropriate for the majority in the night, so they are under the earth outside of great cavities, abundant in matter, and the face of the earth was compacted, lacking pores. When that vapor tries to exit and is unable due to the density of the earth's face, it gathers in itself and moves the earth, and perhaps it had the strength to split the earth, and perhaps the burning parts separate, and terrible sounds occur, and this is the most common cause. And here the chapter has few [causes], such as when inside the earth there are wood [deposits], so great waters flow to them, or a great piece of them collapses, so the air beneath the earth is shaken, and collapse may occur due to the humidity of the mountains and the ease of separation of some parts from others, and due to their dryness and ease of crumbling. And with God is the great victory. The product of the [??] on money in minerals is what we explained regarding the cause of the [??] if heat acts upon it until the mixture between its wet and dry is perfected, as the formation of pumice stone in the formation of mountains: the great barrier when encountering clay with much [??], either at once or over the course of days, it forms a great conduit. And the most common cause is the elevation of the clay masses after its different parts have matured.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage from the Mulakhkhas discusses the natural causes of springs and mountains. The author begins by explaining the conditions for different types of springs: if all conditions are met, flowing springs emerge; if only the third condition is missing, stagnant springs appear; if both the second and third are missing, the water becomes canal water. He then presents a debate about the origin of such waters—whether they are generated from mineral particles scattered underground or from transforming air. The author favors the latter explanation, noting that the earth's surface is often compacted, preventing vapors from escaping. When vapors are trapped, they gather strength, causing earthquakes and sometimes splitting the earth, producing sounds. This is identified as the most common cause. He mentions other causes such as the collapse of underground cavities or the erosion of mountains. The text then shifts to the formation of mountains, specifically through the heat acting upon clay until it solidifies, analogous to the formation of pumice. The mountains arise when clay is consolidated over time, either gradually or suddenly, due to heat and pressure. The emphasis is on the natural, physical causes without invoking supernatural explanation.

Key Terms
العيون السيالة
العيون الراكدة
السبب الأكثرى
الجبال

The type of spring is determined by the presence or absence of specific conditions, and trapped vapor is the primary driver of both earthquakes and spring formation.

Analyzing the Soul as Power, Form, and Completion
Author defines the soul by comparing its aspects as power, form, and completion, favoring completion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the three aspects of the soul (power, form, completion) and justify why 'completion' is the most appropriate definition according to the text.

Core Idea
The soul is best defined as completion, not power or form, because it captures both the principle of actions and the perfection of the species.

In classical Islamic philosophy, defining the soul is essential. The author presents three perspectives – power, form, and completion – and argues why completion is superior. This lesson guides you through each aspect and the reasoning for the preferred definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three relational aspects
Find the three perspectives in the passage: power (quwwa), form (sura), and completion (kamal). Note what each is relative to (e.g., power relative to perception and action).
2
Note the relata for each aspect
For power: relation to reliance on passion for perception. For form: relation to matter to produce a living being. For completion: relation to the deficient body before perfection.
3
Understand why defining as form is problematic
The author says the human soul is not imprinted in matter (غير حالة في المادة), so it cannot be a form in the strict sense. This is why completion is a better term.
4
Understand why defining as power is avoided
The term 'power' is homonymous (مشترك) – it has multiple meanings. Definitions avoid homonymous terms to prevent ambiguity. 'Power' only captures the soul as principle of actions, not its perfecting role.
5
Grasp the distinction between first and second completion
First completion (الكمال الأول) is that by which the species becomes actual (e.g., the soul itself). Second completion (الكمال الثاني) is perhaps the final perfection (e.g., actual knowledge or virtue). The passage indicates this distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قالوا هذه النفس بالقياس إلى أنها تقوى على الفعل الذي هو التوكل على الانفعال للمحسوسات والمعقولات الذي هو الادراك قوة، وبالقياس إلى المادة التي تضم إليها فحصل من اجتماعها نبات أو حيوان أو إنسان صورة، وبالقياس إلى طبيعة المجس إذا كانت ناقصة قبل الفصل فكملت به كمال. وتعريف النفس بالكمال أولى من تعريفها بالصورة لأن النفس الإنسانية غير حالة في المادة فلا تكون صورة فيه وإن كانت كما لا له ومن المقرر أنه ليست القوة الفعلية بأن تارة، وهذا التعريف أولى من التعريف بالفعالية ووقوع لفظ القوة عليها بالاشتراك وهو مجنب عند التعريفات، وإن القوة اسم لها من حيث إنها مبدأ الأفعال، والكمال اسم لها من هذه الحيثية ومن حيث أنها مكملة للنوع. وما يعرف من جميع جهاته أولى مما يعرف من بعض جهاته، فاطراد الكمال أولى لكن الكمال منه أول وهو الذي يصير به النوع نوعا بالفعل ومنه ثان ما سمي العزه الثاني في الفر
Then they said: This soul, in relation to its power to act which is the reliance on passion for the sensibles and intelligibles which is perception, is a power. In relation to the matter which it joins, so from their aggregation arises a plant, animal, or human, it is a form. In relation to the nature of the body if it was deficient before the separation and then was perfected by it, it is a completion. And defining the soul by completion is more appropriate than defining it by form, because the human soul is not imprinted in matter, so it is not a form in it, even if it is like a form. And it is established that the actual power is not such that sometimes? And this definition is more appropriate than the definition by effectiveness, and the use of the term 'power' for it is homonymous, and it is avoided in definitions. For 'power' is a name for it from the perspective that it is the principle of actions, and 'completion' is a name for it from this perspective and from the perspective that it perfects the species. And what is known from all its aspects is more appropriate than what is known from some of its aspects. So the comprehensiveness of 'completion' is more appropriate. But 'completion' has two types: one is that by which the species becomes actual species, and a second which is called the second completion.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a definitional analysis of the soul (nafs). The author presents three perspectives: (1) as a power (quwwa) in relation to its ability to perceive and act; (2) as a form (sura) in relation to the matter it organizes into a living being; (3) as a completion (kamal) in relation to the body that was deficient before receiving it. Then he argues that defining the soul as kamal is superior to defining it as sura, because the human soul is not imprinted (halla) in matter, so it is not a form in the strict sense. He also rejects defining it by 'effectiveness' (fi'aliyya) or by the term 'power' because 'power' is used homonymously. The term 'completion' is better because it captures both aspects: the soul as the principle of actions (mabda' af'al) and as that which perfects the species (mukammila li'l-naw'). Finally, he distinguishes two types of completion: the first completion (al-kamal al-awwal) by which the species becomes actual, and the second completion (al-kamal al-thani) which is perhaps the final perfection. This is a standard philosophical definition of the soul, likely following Aristotle and Ibn Sina, but with critical refinement.

Key Terms
النفس
الكمال
القوة
الصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because form is a more ambiguous term.
C) Because completion includes both power and form.
D) Because the soul is not a substance.

Key Takeaway
The soul is best defined as the first completion of a natural body, because this definition captures both its active and perfective aspects and avoids the problems of alternative definitions.
