As for the first, because when we perceive something we judge it as sweet or bitter or hot or cold. The judge of matters must be aware of them, so there must be something that is exactly the perceiver of these perceived objects with all these perceptions. And because when we imagine forms of perceived objects we judge that imagination to be an imagination of these perceived objects, and that testifies to the existence of [?] then the [?] enable him to judge the empty forms as imagination of this perceived object. Because we say this form is a dream, the truth is realized for you in this human person and by its non-realization in the proximate determinate, so there must be one thing that has the faculty for universals and particulars together. And because if we were mistaken or [?], if the possessor of imagination and the possessor of desire were two things, or [?] not cleared from imagination the hair's solution. Likewise, it is not necessary from imagining Zayd that 'Amr and Shimal appear, so it is established that in man there is one thing from which these perceptions arise. The second: because we know by necessity that in the body there is no bulk or bodily muscle that has a whole of these perceptions accompanying these qualities entirely, because it cannot be bulk or bodily. The thirteenth proposition: if knowledge were necessarily two aspects, it could not be that from each side there is knowledge and from another side there is ignorance so that one human being is knowledgeable and ignorant of the same thing from one aspect? And the meaning of knowledge is nothing but the attainment of the form of the known in the knower, so the knower becomes like that and attains its quiddities. If the locus of this knowledge were a body or bodily, then the conjunction of opposites in one body would be necessary, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs that the soul is not a body. The author begins with the argument from perception: when we perceive something, we judge it as sweet, bitter, hot, or cold. The entity that makes these judgments must be capable of perceiving all these qualities simultaneously. Since a body cannot hold opposite qualities (like hot and cold) at the same time, the perceiver must be non-bodily. The second argument involves imagination and memory: we can imagine a form and later recognize it as a representation of a previously perceived object. This requires a unified faculty that retains both the particular image and the universal concept. If the imagination and desire were separate bodies, they could not interact as they do. The third argument concerns the unity of knowledge: a single human can know something and be ignorant of it from different aspects, but knowledge is the presence of the form of the known in the knower. If the knower were a body, it would have to contain opposite forms simultaneously, which is impossible. Thus, the soul must be non-bodily. These arguments are foundational in Islamic psychology (ilm al-nafs) and rely on the principle that a body cannot receive incompatible qualities at once.

D) Because perception requires a non-physical organ.

Key Takeaway
The soul is not a body because it can simultaneously perceive, imagine, and know opposites, while a body cannot hold opposites.

The Soul's Indivisibility: Razi's Argument and Defense
Dialectical argument for the soul's indivisibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's argument for the soul's indivisibility and analyze his response to the objection that knowledge could be composed of parts.

Core Idea
The soul must be indivisible because it unifies multiple intellectual forms without corruption.

In this passage, Razi presents a twofold argument for the soul's indivisibility and then engages a hypothetical objection. Understanding this dialectical exchange is essential for grasping the Islamic philosophical psychology of the soul as a simple spiritual substance. The passage demonstrates how logical reasoning and linguistic observation jointly support a key theological claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify Razi's conclusion and main arguments
Locate the conclusion that the soul is not divisible. Then find the two distinct arguments: one from the impossibility of opposites in one body, and another from the way we attribute body parts to a single self (e.g., 'my hand').
The second argument is particularly interesting because it uses everyday language as evidence.
2
Analyze the first argument (impossibility of opposites)
Razi contrasts the body, which cannot hold two opposites, with the soul, which can receive multiple intellectual forms without corruption. This suggests the soul is not a material composite like a body.
Consider what 'intellectual health' means in this context.
3
Analyze the second argument (deictic attribution)
Razi points out that we say 'my hand, my leg, my brain' etc., implying a single entity (the soul) that uses these parts as instruments. This argues for a unitary subject behind the parts.
This is a common argument in Islamic psychology for the soul's simplicity.
4
Identify the objection
The objector proposes that knowledge could be composed of parts, each part being knowledge of an aspect, and that aggregation could yield unified knowledge. This challenges the soul's indivisibility by suggesting that the soul's cognitive unity could arise from parts.
The objection targets the apparent unity of consciousness.
5
Examine Razi's response
Razi denies that this would require equal essences for the objects of knowledge. He argues that aggregated parts can produce a whole nature different from the parts, and that knowledge is not tied to the intention of the object. Thus, unified knowledge does not prove the soul is composed of parts.
Assess whether Razi's defense successfully refutes the objection or shifts the burden of proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن اجتماع الضدين في الجسم الواحد وهو محال. والنفس خلاف ذلك فانها إذا قبلت صورة عقلية ونقيت تلك الصورة فيها انداخت بها قوة على تصور سائر المعقولات من غير فساد الأولى. وكلما كانت الصحة العقلية أكثر كانت القوة على توافرها أقوى. وذلك لأنه يضيف الإنسان كل واحد منها إلى نفسه فيقول: يدي ورجلي ودماغي وقلبي، فلا بد من شيء آخر تكون هذه الأشياء آلة له وهو المطلوب. وأما الذي احتجوا به أولاً فتقول: لم لا يجوز أن ينضم العلم إلى أجزاء كل واحد منها يكون علماً بذلك؟ قوله: يلزم أن يكون كل ما له نسبة واحدة في العلم متساوياً في الماهية لو في جميع العوارض، والأول مسلم ولا امتناع فيه، والثاني ممنوع إلا أن يقيموا دلالة على أن وجود العلم إذا كان على هذا الوجه يقتضي أن تكون متعلقاته مخالفة له في سائر العوارض، وهم إلى الآن ما فعلوا ذلك. ثم إن وجود العلم لا يجوز أن يكون متعلقاً بنية ذلك المعلوم، ولكن يمكن أن تحصل عند اجتماع تلك الأمور هوية كلية تخالف كل واحد منها, فلم لا يعقل مسلمة العلم وبهذا الوجه؟
For the combination of two opposites in one body is impossible. And the soul is contrary to that, because when it receives an intellectual form and that form is preserved in it, it gains power to perceive the rest of the intelligibles without corruption of the first. And the more intellectual health, the stronger the power to have them. That is because man attributes each of these to himself, saying: my hand, my leg, my brain, my heart, so there must be something else for which these things are instruments, and that is what is sought. As for what they first argued, we say: why is it not permissible for knowledge to be attached to parts, each of which is knowledge of that? He said: it would necessitate that everything having a single relation in knowledge be equal in essence, if not in all accidents. The first is granted and there is no impossibility in it, but the second is denied unless they prove that the existence of knowledge in this manner requires that its objects be different from it in all accidents, and they have not done that yet. Then, the existence of knowledge cannot be attached to the intention of that object, but it is possible that upon the aggregation of those things, a single whole nature is obtained that is different from each one. So why not conceive of knowledge as being whole in this manner?
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage is a critical dialectical exchange. The author argues for the indivisibility of the soul, using the impossibility of two opposites in one body as a starting point. He contrasts this with the soul, which can receive multiple intellectual forms without corruption. He then provides a linguistic argument: we attribute body parts to ourselves, implying a single self that uses them. The second part responds to an objection: if knowledge were composed of parts, each part would be knowledge of an aspect, but the author denies that this leads to equality of objects. He suggests that aggregated parts can yield a whole different from its components, allowing for unified knowledge. The argument is dense and relies on the notion that the soul is a simple substance. The text is heavily corrupted in the original OCR, so the reconstruction is tentative.

Analyzing a Dialectical Exchange on Point and Quiddity
Author refutes an objection about points receiving intellectual forms, arguing quiddities may differ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection and refutation in a dialectical argument about quiddity and assess the logical move of distinguishing quiddity from accidents.

Core Idea
Points may share a name but differ in quiddity—so a universal property need not apply uniformly.

In this passage, the author engages in a typical dialectical (jadal) move: an objector claims that if one point can receive an intellectual form, then every point can, because points seem identical. The refutation counters by suggesting that points may have distinct quiddities even if they share the same definition or accidents. This lesson will walk you through the logical structure of this exchange, which is foundational for debates about universals and particulars in Islamic philosophy and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objector's Claim
First, locate the objector's main assertion: that if a point were to receive an intellectual form, every point would receive it. This is based on the assumption that all points are identical in quiddity because they share the definition of 'indivisible with position'.
The objector is using a universal generalization: what holds for one holds for all under the same species.
2
Extract the Refutation's Key Distinction
The refutation introduces a distinction between the common definition or accidents of a point and its actual quiddity (essence). It argues that the definition 'indivisible with position' may not exhaust the quiddity of each point, so points can have different quiddities.
This is a move from shared accidents to possible difference in essence.
3
Reconstruct the Argument in Categorical Form
Try to represent the reasoning as a categorical syllogism: Major premise: 'Everything that shares the same definition of point must share the same quiddity' (implicitly claimed by objector). Minor premise: 'All points share the definition.' Conclusion: 'All points have the same quiddity.' The refutation rejects the major premise.
The refutation does not present a syllogism but its logical impact is to deny the universal premise.
4
Evaluate the Strength of the Refutation
Consider whether the refutation successfully shows that points could differ in quiddity. Does the fact that 'quiddity is outside the essence and returns to it' really prevent universalization? This step requires understanding the technical meaning of mahiyya and its independence from accidents.
The passage is cryptic due to textual corruption; consult the original for precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If you grant that they share the entire quiddity, then why is it not said that the condition for the point to be receptive is that the volume which...

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange: an objection and its refutation concerning the capacity of a point to receive an intellectual form. The objection claims that if one point could receive an intellectual form, then every point would equally be able to, because points seem identical in their definition as indivisible entities. The refutation counters by distinguishing between the common name 'point' and the actual quiddity (mahiyya) of each point. It argues that what is said about the point—that it has no substance or definition?—indicates that quiddity is not exhausted by those descriptions. Therefore, it is possible for each point to have a distinct quiddity even if they share the same accidents. Hence, the objection fails because the universal receptivity does not follow. The passage guards against the misunderstanding that all points are identical in essence.

Animal Recognition Proves Soul Persistence
Argument from animal recognition to soul persistence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the immediate inference from an animal's recognition of a past event to the persistence of its soul.

Core Idea
Animal recognition of past events reveals the soul's identity across time.

The author of the Mulakhkhas addresses a key philosophical problem: how can a living being remain the same individual when its body and attributes change? His initial approach questions whether we can be certain of persistence based on outward form. He then presents a stronger argument grounded in animal behavior: an animal's ability to recognize a person who hit it yesterday entails knowledge of its own self's existence yesterday. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the problem
The author notes that a specific horse persists from birth to death despite its bodily parts and attributes changing. This is the puzzle: what accounts for the individual's identity?
2
2. Reject the weak approach
The author first considers whether we can know the horse's persistence through its perceived structure (بنية) and form (هيئة). He concludes that we cannot be certain of persistence based on these.
3
3. Adopt the behavioral approach
Instead, he establishes the minor premise through animal behavior: an animal recognizes its owner, points to the person who hit it yesterday, and avoids that person today. This shows it knows that this person is the one who hit it yesterday.
4
4. Analyze the inference to soul persistence
The animal's knowledge that 'this person hit me yesterday' involves a relation between the person and the animal's own self. For this relation to be known, the animal must know both relata – the person and its own self – and that the relation holds over time. Therefore, the animal knows that its soul (self) existed yesterday.
This is an immediate inference: from knowing the relation to knowing the persistence of the self.
5
5. Conclude the necessity of persistence
The author deduces that the animal necessarily knows the persistence of its identity (بقاء هويته). This proves the soul remains identical despite bodily changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومسألة النفس: الفرس المعين باقية من أول العمر إلى آخره مع أن أجزاء بدنه وصفاته تتبدل. فنقول: إني لا أعرف من الفرس إلا هذه البنية وهذه الهيئة، ولا يمكن القطع بقائها. فالعلم بالفرس لا يمكن ادعاء بقائه. والذي يمكن ادعاء بقائه لا أعلمه. فأولى تقرير الصغرى بطريق آخَر: وهو أن الحيوان يتعرف صاحبه وغيره، ويشار إلى الشخص الذي ضربه أمس ويقصد، وينفي عن الشخص الذي ضربه اليوم، وكل ذلك يدل على كونه عالما بأن هذا الشخص هو الذي ضربه أمس. وعلمه بأن هذا الأنا هو الذي ضربه أمس يتضمن علمه بأن نفسه هي التي كانت موجودة بالأمس. لأن تعبيره عن هذا الإنسان بأنه ضربه أمس يتعلق بنسبة خصوصة بين هذا الإنسان ونفسه في الماضي. والعلم بالإضافة مفتقر إلى عالم به. فالحيوان لا بد أن يعلم الآن كونه موجودا بالأمس. فيثبت أن الحيوان يعلم بالضرورة بقاء هويته، وذلك يفيد المطلوب.
And the problem of the soul: the specific horse persists from the beginning of life to its end despite the parts of its body and its attributes changing. We say: I do not know from the horse except this structure and this form, and it is not possible to be certain of its persistence. So knowledge of the horse cannot claim its persistence. And that whose persistence can be claimed I do not know. So it is better to establish the minor premise in another way: that the animal recognizes its owner and others, and points to the person who hit it yesterday and intends, and denies about the person who hit it today, and all that indicates that it knows that this person is the one who hit it yesterday. And its knowledge that this self (ana) is the one who hit it yesterday entails its knowledge that its soul was existing yesterday. Because its expression that this man hit it yesterday relates to a specific relation between this man and itself in the past. And knowledge of relation requires knowledge of the relata. So the animal must know now that it existed yesterday. Thus it is established that the animal necessarily knows the persistence of its identity, and that achieves what is required.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage addresses the problem of the soul's persistence. The author begins by noting that a specific horse persists throughout life even though its bodily parts and attributes change. He first considers whether we can claim knowledge of the horse's persistence based on its perceived form and structure, but finds that uncertain. Instead, he establishes the minor premise of an argument through another route: the animal's ability to recognize its owner and distinguish others. For example, an animal points to the person who hit it yesterday and shuns that person today, while not shunning someone else. This shows the animal knows that this very person is the one who hit it yesterday. Such knowledge implies the animal knows that its own self (nafs) was present yesterday. The reasoning is that the relation 'hitting' between the person and itself requires knowledge of both relata—the person and its own self—across time. Therefore, the animal necessarily knows the persistence of its identity (huwiyya). This serves as a proof that the soul remains identical despite bodily changes.

Key Terms
نفس
هوية
بقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The animal must remember the person's face exactly.
B) The animal must know that its own self existed yesterday.
C) The animal must have a concept of time.
D) The animal must have a soul that is separate from the body.

Key Takeaway
The animal's recognition of a past event implies its knowledge of its own self's persistence, proving the soul remains identical despite bodily changes.

The Identity of Essence and Existence in the Necessary Being
Author argues that wajib al-wujud's essence is identical to its existence, unlike contingent beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that the Necessary Being's essence must be identical to its existence, and explain why a distinct quiddity would entail contingency.

Core Idea
For the Necessary Being, existence is not an accident—essence is existence itself.

This passage tackles one of the most profound claims in Islamic philosophy: that the Necessary Being (wajib al-wujud) has no quiddity separate from existence. The author argues that if its essence were distinct, that essence would be capable of existence and non-existence, making it contingent and dependent on a cause—contradicting necessity. Let's work through this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three possibilities for any essence
The passage begins by considering a being's essence (haqiqa) in itself. Two possibilities are examined: either it does not admit non-existence, or it does. If it admits existence, then it is capable of both existence and non-existence, which defines contingency. This sets up the dilemma for the Necessary Being.
The 'three aspects' mentioned likely refer to: (1) essence alone, (2) essence considered with existence, (3) essence considered with non-existence.
2
Identify the consequence of a distinct essence
The author states that if the Necessary Being had an essence other than existence, that essence would be subject to non-existence (i.e., it could potentially not exist). Such an essence would then be contingent (mumkin) and would require an external cause (mu'aththir).
This is a reductio ad absurdum: a distinct quiddity leads to contingency, which contradicts the definition of the Necessary Being.
3
Extract the conditional argument
The core logical structure is a hypothetical syllogism: If the Necessary Being has a quiddity distinct from existence, then it is contingent. But the Necessary Being cannot be contingent. Therefore, it has no quiddity distinct from existence. This is a modus tollens.
Formally: If P (distinct quiddity) then Q (contingency). Not Q, therefore not P.
4
Apply the principle to the Necessary Being
The author concludes that the Necessary Being's essence (haqiqa) is its very existence (nafs wujudihi). Since existence itself cannot accept non-existence, the Necessary Being is eternally existent. Any other view would make necessity and contingency share the same essence, which is impossible.
This doctrine is known as 'identity of essence and existence in God' (ittihad al-mahiyya wal-wujud).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن المالك [؟] لذلك لوجوه ثلاثة في حقيقته من حيث هي هي إن لم يكن فيها قبول العدم استحال العدم عليها وإن كان وهي أيضا قابلة للوجود فهي قابلة لها فهي ممكنة تفتقر إلى المؤثر. هذا [؟] كل عدم بعد الوجود متجدد وكل متجدد ممكن وكل ممكن فله سبب. [؟] فكل ما يفتقر إلى العدم فهو ممكن. فإذا قلنا واجب الوجود حقيقته نفس وجوده قلنا الوجود لا يقبل العدم. [؟] لا حقيقة له سوى الوجود أولى أن لا يقبل العدم. [؟] حقيقة مخالفة لحقائق الممكنات [؟] لزم اشتراك الوجوب والإمكان [؟] محال.
Indeed, the owner [?] of that has three aspects in its essence, in itself: if it has no possibility of non-existence, then non-existence is impossible for it; and if it is also capable of existence, then it is capable of it, so it is contingent and needs an effecter. This [?] every non-existence after existence is renewed, and every renewed is possible, and every possible has a cause. [?] So everything that is subject to non-existence is possible. So if we say the necessary of existence, its essence is its existence, we say existence does not accept non-existence. [?] It has no essence other than existence, so it is more appropriate not to accept non-existence. [?] An essence contrary to the essences of possibles [?] would necessitate commonality between necessity and contingency [?] impossible.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage explores the nature of the necessary being (wajib al-wujud). The author presents an argument that the necessary being cannot have a quiddity (mahiyya) separate from existence. He begins by considering the essence (haqiqa) of the necessary being in itself. If that essence cannot admit non-existence, then non-existence is impossible for it. But if it can admit existence (i.e., is capable of existence), then it is contingent (mumkin) and requires a cause. The author then states that every non-existence after existence is a temporal event (huduth), and everything temporal is possible and has a cause. Anything that is subject to non-existence is possible. Therefore, if we say that the necessary being's essence is its very existence (haqiqatuhu nafs wujudihi), then existence cannot accept non-existence. Since it has no essence other than existence, it is more fitting that it does not accept non-existence. If it had an essence distinct from existence, that essence would be like the essences of contingent beings, leading to the absurdity that necessity and contingency share the same essence, which is impossible. Thus, the necessary being's essence is identical to its existence.

Key Terms
الحقيقة
الوجود
الممكن
الواجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if the necessary being had a quiddity distinct from existence, what would follow?
A) It would be eternal.
B) It would be contingent.
C) It would be impossible.
D) It would be necessary.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being's essence is its existence—any separation would imply contingency, contradicting its necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar reductio argument for the proposition: 'The Necessary Being has no attributes distinct from its essence.' Use the same logical form as in the passage.
?
Identify the quiddity and existence in the following statement: 'The human is a rational animal.' Explain why this quiddity is distinct from its existence, and why that marks it as contingent.

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
5 min
Self-Knowledge in God: Refuting the Distinction Objection
Author refutes objection against divine self-knowledge by analogy with human self-awareness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the objection that divine self-knowledge requires a real distinction between knower and known, using the analogical method exemplified by human self-awareness.

Core Idea
A thing can be both subject and object of knowledge without contradiction.

This passage presents a key dialectical exchange in Islamic theology concerning God's knowledge of His own essence. The opponent argues that knowledge requires a distinction between knower and known, making God's self-knowledge impossible. The author refutes this by appealing to the evident fact of human self-awareness, demonstrating that the conditional premise is false.

Guided Path
1
State the opponent's claim
Identify the core objection: knowledge requires a distinction between العاقل (the intellect) and المعقول (the intelligible), so a simple being like God cannot be both.
The opponent assumes a real distinction is necessary.
2
Extract the conditional reasoning
The opponent uses a conditional: if knowledge requires distinction, then a simple entity cannot know itself. Since God is simple, He cannot know Himself.
This is a modus ponens form: if P then Q, P, therefore Q.
3
Find the author's counterexample
The author points to human self-knowledge: a single human being intellects his own essence while being one entity, showing that the conditional premise is not universally true.
The counterexample falsifies the conditional.
4
Evaluate the refutation's validity
Assess whether the refutation succeeds: human self-knowledge is an actual case where the antecedent (distinction) is false but the consequent (knowledge) holds, so the conditional is false. Thus the objection collapses.
The argument is logically sound; it uses a counterexample to deny the major premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أنكر علمه تعالى بذاته لزم أن لا يكون الله عالما بنفسه، لأن نسبة العلم بين العاقل والمعقول نسبة مخصوصة لا تتحقق إلا بتغاير بينهما، والله واحد بسيط، فكونه عاقلا ومعقولا معا محال. والجواب: أن هذا منقوض بعلم الإنسان بنفسه، فإن الإنسان يعقل ذاته وهو واحد، فلا استحالة في كون الشيء قابلا وفاعلا معا.
Whoever denies His knowledge of His essence necessarily entails that God is not knowing of Himself, because the relation of knowledge between the intellect and the intelligible is a specific relation that only occurs by distinction between them, while God is one and simple, so His being both intellect and intelligible simultaneously is impossible. The answer: that this is refuted by man's knowledge of himself, for man intellects his own essence while he is one, so there is no impossibility in a thing being both subject and object simultaneously.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange concerning God's knowledge of His own essence. The opponent argues that for knowledge to occur, there must be a distinction between the knower (العاقل) and the known (المعقول). Since God is absolutely one and simple, such a distinction cannot obtain; hence God cannot know Himself. The author refutes this by pointing to the case of human self-knowledge: a human being can know his own essence while being one and the same entity. If it is possible for a human, it is even more so for God. Thus the objection is invalid. The argument demonstrates the logical principle that a thing can be both agent and patient (فاعل وقابل) without contradiction.

Key Terms
عاقل ('āqil): One who intellects; the subject of intellection. [Note: In this text, it refers to the intellecting agent, distinct from the intelligible.]
معقول (ma'qūl): The object of intellection; what is intellectually apprehended. [Note: No significant shift; stable across the tradition.]
نسبة (nisba): Relation; here the specific relation between intellect and intelligible that supposedly requires duality. [Note: Used in logic and ontology; no shift in meaning within this context.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that God cannot know Himself because knowledge requires a distinction between knower and known?
A) By asserting that God is beyond such distinctions.
B) By pointing out that human beings can know themselves while being one entity.
C) By arguing that God's knowledge is not like human knowledge.
D) By denying the premise that knowledge requires a distinction.

Key Takeaway
Human self-knowledge demonstrates that being both knower and known in a single entity is not impossible, thereby refuting the objection against divine self-knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the objection: 'If God knows the universe, then the universe must be distinct from God; therefore God cannot know the universe directly.' Refute this objection using the same analogical method as the passage, i.e., by citing a case where a thing knows something without being fully distinct from it. Provide an example from Islamic theology or daily life.
?
Construct a conditional argument that the opponent in the passage might have used, and then identify the point at which the author's counterexample breaks the conditional.
